Classification of Land and the Dispute on Reviving Dead Land
States land classification rules and an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for classifying land as ushr or kharaj and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on classifying revived dead land.

Core Idea
Revived dead land may be ushr or kharaj depending on region (Abu Yusuf) or water source (Muhammad).

Land classification in Hanafi fiqh determines whether a Muslim landowner pays 'ushr (a tithe on produce) or kharaj (a land tax). The rules for original acquisition are clear: land taken peacefully or divided among spoilers is 'ushr; land conquered by force and left with its inhabitants is kharaj. But what about land that was dead and then revived? Here, two giants of the Hanafi school—Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad—disagree on the decisive factor: is it the adjoining region or the water source? This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the basic classification rules
Read the opening lines: land whose people accepted Islam or was conquered by force and divided becomes ushr land; land conquered by force and left with its people becomes kharaj land.
This is the foundation from Al-Quduri.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text presents two views: Abu Yusuf classifies revived dead land by its region (hayz); Muhammad classifies it by the water source used for irrigation.
Both are respected Hanafi imams; the text does not resolve the dispute.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf uses the precedent of Basra, which is ushr by consensus, and argues that the region determines the status. The term 'hayz' means the adjoining tract.
His method is qiyas with the known ruling.
4
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad distinguishes based on the water source: public rivers (Tigris, Euphrates) or new wells yield ushr land; rivers dug by non-Muslims (e.g., King's River) yield kharaj land.
He relies on the hadith of Al-Ala ibn al-Hadrami and a general principle of water ownership.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The same plot of revived land could be taxed differently depending on which view a mufti adopts. Later Hanafi practice often follows Muhammad's view.
The dispute affects the tax burden on the owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِ الْعُشْرَ، وَمِنَ الْمُشْرِكِ الْخَرَاجَ ، سنن ابن ماجه، بَابُ الْعُشْرِ وَالْخَرَاج، نمبر 1831) وجه: (۳) قول التابعى وَكُلُّ أَرْضِ أَسْلَمَ أَهْلُهَا عَلَيْهَا قَالَ كَتَبَ عُمَرُ بْنُ عَبْدِ الْعَزِيزِ رحمه الله إِلَى عَبْدِ الحَمِيدِ بْن عَبْدِ الرَّحْمَنِ، فَذَكَرَهُ، فَقَالَ فِيهِ: وَلَا خَرَاجَ عَلَى مَنْ أَسْلَمَ مِنْ أَهْلِ

كِتَابُ السّير
وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ إِنْ أَحْيَاهَا بِثْرِ حَفَرَهَا أَوْ عَيْنِ اسْتَخْرَجَهَا أَوْ مَاءِ دِجْلَةَ أَوْ الْفُرَاتِ أَوْ الْأَنْهَارِ الْعِظَامِ الَّتي لا يَمْلِكُهَا أَحَدٌ فَهِيَ عُشْرِيَّةٌ وَإِنْ أَحْيَاهَا بِمَاءِ الْأَنْهَارِ الَّتِي احْتَفَرَهَا الْأَعَاجِمُ كَنَهْرٍ الْمَلِكِ وَنَهْرٍ يَزْدَجْرِدْ فَهِيَ خَرَاجِيَّةٌ
Book of Siyar. Every land whose people accepted Islam upon it or was conquered by force and divided among the spoilers, it is ushr land. And every land conquered by force and its people were left in place, it is kharaj land. And whoever revives dead land: according to Abu Yusuf, it is considered by its region; if it is from the region of kharaj land, it is kharaji; if from the region of ushr land, it is ushri. And Basra, according to us, is ushri by consensus of the Companions. ... Proofs: (3) Statement of the follower: 'And all the land of Sawad is owned by its people.' He said: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz wrote to Abd al-Hamid ibn Abd al-Rahman, mentioning that, and said in it: 'No kharaj on whoever accepts Islam from the people of the land.' ... (2) Hadith: from Al-Ala ibn al-Hadrami, said: The Messenger of Allah sent me to Bahrain or to Hajar, and I would come to a garden between brothers, one of them accepts Islam, and I would take ushr from the Muslim and kharaj from the polytheist. ... Principles: The condition of kharaj is that the residents are left on their own religion, and on ushr land ushr is taken. Vocabulary: yuqarr: to keep in place; Sawad al-Iraq: a place name; anwatan wa qahran: by force; ru'usihim: themselves. Book of Siyar. And Muhammad said: If he revives it with a well he dug or a spring he extracted or water from the Tigris, Euphrates, or major rivers that no one owns, then it is ushri. And if he revives it with water from rivers that the Persians dug, like the King's River and the River of Yazdgard, then it is kharaji.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, with its commentary, covers two major topics: the classification of land as ushr or kharaj based on its acquisition, and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the status of revived dead land. The author first states the rule: land whose inhabitants accepted Islam peacefully, or land conquered by force and then divided among the spoilers, is ushr land (subject to ushr tax). Land conquered by force but left in the hands of its original inhabitants is kharaj land (subject to kharaj tax). Then the question arises: if someone revives dead land (mawat), how is it classified? Abu Yusuf says it is classified by the adjoining land: if the dead land is adjacent to kharaj land, it becomes kharaji; if adjacent to ushr land, it becomes ushri. He cites the example of Basra, which by consensus of the Companions is ushr land. Muhammad, on the other hand, distinguishes based on the water source used for irrigation. If the water comes from a newly dug well or spring, or from major rivers like the Tigris or Euphrates (which are public property), the revived land is ushri. If the water comes from rivers dug by the Persians (like the King's River or Yazdgard River), the land is kharaji. The text then provides multiple proofs: hadith of Al-Ala ibn al-Hadrami, and the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. The commentary also explains that kharaj is imposed on lands whose residents are allowed to retain their religion, while ushr is taken from Muslims.

Key Terms
أرض عشر (ard ushr): Land subject to ushr tax (10% or 5% of produce) because its inhabitants are Muslim or it was conquered peacefully and divided.
أرض خراج (ard kharaj): Land subject to kharaj tax, usually conquered by force and left with non-Muslim inhabitants.
أرض موات (ard mawat): Dead land, uncultivated and not owned, which can be revived by someone.
عنوة (anwah): By force; refers to land conquered through military conquest.
حيز (hayz): Region or vicinity; used by Abu Yusuf to determine land classification based on what is adjacent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if a person revives dead land using water from the Euphrates River (a major public river), the land is classified as:
A) Kharaji land
B) Ushri land
C) Depends on the adjoining land
D) Uncultivable

Key Takeaway
The classification of revived dead land in the Hanafi school is disputed: Abu Yusuf uses the adjoining region; Muhammad uses the water source, with the latter view often preferred.

beginner
6 min
Kharaj Rates and the Imam's Authority to Adjust
States the kharaj rates imposed by Umar on Sawad land and the principle of reduction by the imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the kharaj rates set by Umar on Sawad lands and explain the imam's authority to reduce kharaj based on land capacity.

Core Idea
The imam sets kharaj according to land capacity and may reduce it if the land cannot bear the tax.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn presents the classical Hanafi position on kharaj taxation. It records the rates established by Caliph Umar on the agricultural lands of Sawad and affirms the principle that the imam has the authority to adjust the tax according to the land's productive capacity. Understanding this ruling is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to state taxation and the balance between revenue and justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the kharaj rates for specific crops as set by Umar and the general principle of reduction by the imam.
The rates are for irrigated land: wheat/barley (1 dirham + 1 qafiz), vegetables (5 dirhams), grapes/dates (10 dirhams).
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proof: the actions and statements of Umar and Uthman ibn Hunayf, which are qawl sahabi.
The text cites narrations from Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling: the sahaba explicitly set rates and emphasized not overburdening the land, establishing the principle of capacity-based adjustment.
Umar's instruction to not overload the land is key.
4
Apply the principle
For crops not mentioned, the imam assesses capacity and sets a rate; if the land cannot meet it, he reduces it.
The Urdu commentary reiterates that kharaj is based on produce and reduction is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْخَرَاجُ الَّذِي وَضَعَهُ عُمَرُ بْنُ الْخَطَّابِ - رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ - عَلَى أَهْلِ السَّوَادِ فِي كُلِّ جَرِيبٍ يَبْلُغُهُ الْمَاءُ قَفِيزُهَا شَمَيٌّ وَهُوَ الصَّاعُ وَدِرْهَمُ وَفِي جَرِيبِ الرَّطْبَةِ خَمْسَةُ دَرَاهِمَ وَفِي جَرِيبِ الْكَرْمِ الْمُتَصِل وَالنَّخْلِ الْمُتَّصِلِ عَشَرَةُ دَرَاهِمَ
● وَمَا سِوَى ذَلِكَ مِنْ الْأَصْنَافِ يُوَضَعُ عَلَيْهَا بِحَسَبِ الطَّاقَةِ فَإِنْ لَمْ تُطِقْ مَا وُضِعَ عَلَيْهَا نَقَصَهَا الْإِمَامُ د وجه : (۱) قول الصحابي وَالْخَرَاجُ الَّذِي وَضَعَهُ عُمَرُ \ أَنَّ عُمَرَ بْنَ الْخَطَّابِ، بَعَثَ عُثْمَانَ بْنَ حُنَيْفٍ عَلَى السَّوَادِ فَوَضَعَ عَلَى كُلِ جَرِيبٍ عَامِرٍ أَوْ عَامِرٍ يَنَالُهُ الْمَاءُ دِرْهَمَا، وَقَفِيرًا، يَعْنِي الْحِنْطَةَ وَالشَّعِيرَ ، وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ الْكَرْمِ عَشْرَةَ، وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ الرِّطَابِ خَمْسَةَ،(سنن مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، مَا قَالُوا فِي الْخُمُسِ وَالْخَرَاجِ كَيْفَ يُوَضَعُ ، غر 32716/ سنن بيهقي، بَابُ قَدْرِ الْخَرَاجِ الَّذِي وُضِعَ عَلَى السَّوَادِ، نمبر 18383)
و وجه : (۱) قول الصحابي وَمَا سِوَى ذَلِكَ مِنْ الْأَصْنَافِ يُوَضَعُ عَلَيْهَا \ » رَأَيْتُ عُمَرَ بْنَ الْخَطَّابِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يُصَابَ بِأَيَّامٍ بِالْمَدِينَةِ، وَقَفَ عَلَى حُذَيْفَةَ بْنِ الْيَمَانِ وَعُثْمَانَ بْنِ حُنَيْفٍ قَالَ: كَيْفَ فَعَلْتُمَا، أَتَكَافَانِ أَنْ تَكُونَا قَدْ حَمَلْتُمَا الْأَرْضَ مَا لَا تُطِيقُ؟ قَالَا: حَمَّلْنَاهَا أَمْرًا هِيَ لَهُ مُطِيقَةٌ، مَا فِيهَا كَبِيرُ فَضْلِ. قَالَ: انْظُرَا أَنْ تَكُونَا حَمَّلْتُمَا الْأَرْضَ مَا لَا تُطِيقُ، قَالَ: قَالَا: لَا، (بخاري، قِصَّةُ الْبَيْعَةِ وَالاتِفَاقُ عَلَى عُثْمَانَ بْن عَفَّانَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ، نمبر 3600)
وجه : (۲) قول التابعى وَمَا سِوَى ذَلِكَ مِنْ الْأَصْنَافِ يُوَضَعُ عَلَيْهَا فَوَضَعَ عُثْمَانُ عَلَى الْجَرِيبِ مِنَ الْكَرْمِ عَشَرَةَ دَرَاهِمَ، وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ النَّخْلِ ثَمَانِيَةَ دَرَاهِمَ وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ الْقَصَبِ سِتَّةَ دَرَاهِمَ، يَعْنِي الرُّطَبَةَ، وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ الْبُرِ أَرْبَعَةَ دَرَاهِمَ، وَعَلَى جَرِيبِ السَّعِيرِ دِرْهَمَيْنِ،(مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، مَا قَالُوا فِي الْخُمُسِ وَالْخَراج كَيْفَ يُوَضَعُ، غمبر 32714/ سنن بيهقي، بَابُ قَدْرِ الْخَرَاج الَّذِي وُضِعَ عَلَى السَّوَادِ، نمبر 18382)
اصول: خراج زمین کی پیداوار کے مطابق لازم کیا جائے گا، اور متعین خراج کے بعد کمی کی جاسکتی ہے۔
And the kharaj that Umar ibn al-Khattab placed on the people of Sawad: for every jarib that water reaches, its qafiz is one (a measure) and it is the sa' and a dirham. For a jarib of vegetables, five dirhams. For a jarib of connected grapevines and connected date palms, ten dirhams. And for other types, the imam imposes according to capacity; if it cannot bear what was imposed, the imam reduces it. Proof (1): Statement of the Companion: Umar ibn al-Khattab sent Uthman ibn Hunayf to Sawad, and he placed on every cultivated jarib that water reaches: one dirham and one qafiz, meaning wheat and barley; on a jarib of grapes: ten dirhams; on a jarib of vegetables: five dirhams. ... And proof (2): Statement of the follower: Uthman placed on a jarib of grapes: ten dirhams; on a jarib of dates: eight dirhams; on a jarib of sugarcane: six dirhams, meaning the vegetable; on a jarib of wheat: four dirhams; on a jarib of barley: two dirhams. Principles: Kharaj will be imposed according to the land's produce, and after setting the kharaj, reduction is possible.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage details the historical kharaj rates set by Caliph Umar on the lands of Sawad (Iraq), and establishes the principle that the imam may adjust the tax based on the land's capacity. The rates are: for irrigated land growing wheat or barley, 1 dirham plus 1 qafiz (a measure of grain) per jarib; for vegetables, 5 dirhams; for grapes and date palms, 10 dirhams. For other crops, the imam sets a rate according to ability, and if the land cannot yield that amount, he reduces it. The text provides proofs from the actions of Umar and Uthman, showing that the sahaba applied these rates and emphasized not overburdening the land. The Urdu commentary restates that kharaj is based on produce and can be reduced after assessment.

Key Terms
خراج (kharaj): A land tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (and sometimes on Muslim-owned kharaj land) based on land productivity.
جرير (jarib): A unit of land area (roughly equivalent to 60 square zira').
قفيز (qafiz): A measure of capacity for grain (approximately 12 liters or varied).
طاقة (taqa): Capacity; the land's ability to produce enough to pay the kharaj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the kharaj rate for a jarib of connected grapevines as set by Umar?
A) 5 dirhams
B) 8 dirhams
C) 10 dirhams
D) 1 dirham and 1 qafiz

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is imposed according to land's capacity; the imam has authority to reduce it if the land cannot bear the burden.

Ushri vs Kharaji Land Based on Irrigation Water
Ruling on tax classification of revived land by water source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's ruling on the classification of revived land based on water source and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Land revived with ownerless water is ushri; with water from owned canals is kharaji.

This passage from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani establishes a key principle in Hanafi land tax law. The tax status of revived dead land depends on the ownership of the water used for irrigation, not on the type of crop grown. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct tax classification in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act: reviving dead land
The ruling applies to 'ihya al-mawat'—reviving barren land that is not owned. The reviver brings the land into cultivation.
This act is encouraged in Islam and can lead to ownership if conditions are met.
2
Identify the water source
Determine whether the irrigation water comes from an ownerless source (well dug by reviver, spring, major rivers like Tigris/Euphrates) or from canals owned by others (e.g., canals dug by Persians like the King's Canal).
Ownerless sources are not subject to private ownership; owned sources are constructed by others.
3
Apply the classification rule
If the water source is ownerless, the land is 'ushri' (subject to 'ushr tax). If the water comes from owned canals, the land is 'kharaji' (subject to kharaj land tax).
Muhammad's view is stated; other Hanafi imams may have differed.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf may have had alternative criteria for classification, but Muhammad's view is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon in later Hanafi works.
The text only gives Muhammad's ruling; we note the difference for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ إِنْ أَحْيَاهَا بِثْرِ حَفَرَهَا أَوْ عَيْنِ اسْتَخْرَجَهَا أَوْ مَاءِ دِجْلَةَ أَوْ الْفُرَاتِ أَوْ الْأَنْهَارِ الْعِظَامِ الَّتي لا يَمْلِكُهَا أَحَدٌ فَهِيَ عُشْرِيَّةٌ وَإِنْ أَحْيَاهَا بِمَاءِ الْأَنْهَارِ الَّتِي احْتَفَرَهَا الْأَعَاجِمُ كَنَهْرٍ الْمَلِكِ وَنَهْرٍ يَزْدَجْرِدْ فَهِيَ خَرَاجِيَّةٌ
And Muhammad said: If he revives it with a well he dug, or a spring he extracted, or water of the Tigris or the Euphrates or the great rivers that no one owns, then it is ushri; and if he revives it with water of rivers that the Persians dug, like the King's River and the River of Yazdgird, then it is kharaji.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is a ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the classification of dead land that is revived (ihya al-mawat). The determining factor is the source of irrigation water. If the water comes from a source that is not privately owned—like water from a well dug by the reviver, a spring, or from major rivers like the Tigris and Euphrates—then the land is considered 'ushri (subject to 'ushr tax of 10% or 5% on produce). If the water comes from man-made canals dug by non-Muslims (the Persians), such as the King's Canal or the canal of Yazdgird, then the land is kharaji (subject to kharaj, a fixed land tax). This distinction is based on the ownership of the water source: ownerless water yields ushri land; water from property owned by others yields kharaji land. This is the view of Muhammad; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf may have different positions, but the text only states Muhammad's opinion.

Key Terms
عُشْرِيَّة (ushriyyah): Land subject to 'ushr tax (10% or 5% on produce).
خَرَاجِيَّة (kharajiyyah): Land subject to kharaj (a fixed land tax).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, if land is revived using water from the Euphrates, what type of land is it?
A) Kharaji
B) Ushri
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Land revived with ownerless water is ushri; with water from owned canals is kharaji.

Kharaj Rates Set by Umar: A Historical Precedent
Historical rates of kharaj on various crops, with evidence from Umar's practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the kharaj rates imposed by Umar on different types of agricultural land and identify the proof (qawl al-ṣaḥābī) that establishes these rates in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Umar's kharaj rates for grain, fodder, and vineyards set a binding precedent in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines the historical kharaj rates imposed by Caliph Umar on the agricultural lands of Sawad (Iraq). These rates, recorded in the works of early traditionists, serve as an authoritative precedent (qawl al-ṣaḥābī) within the Hanafi school. Understanding these rates is essential for grasping the classical Hanafi position on land taxation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the land types and their rates
From the text, list the three types of land: irrigated grain land (jarīb yablughuhu al-mā’), green fodder (raṭbah), and continuous vineyards and palm trees (karm muttaṣil wa nakhl muttaṣil). Note the rate for each: 1 qafiz and 1 dirham for grain, 5 dirhams for fodder, 10 dirhams for vines/palms.
The term 'shami' is clarified as the sa' measure.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is the report from Umar via ʿUthmān ibn Ḥunayf, recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi. This is a qawl al-ṣaḥābī, which the Hanafi school considers a binding source in the absence of conflicting evidence.
The full hadith is provided in the dalil block.
3
Understand the legal status
In Hanafi fiqh, the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, especially Umar, is a strong precedent. The rates are not merely historical but serve as a reference for kharaj assessment, though the Imam may adjust them based on istiḥsān (juristic preference).
This flexibility is important to avoid treating the rates as immutable.
4
Note the practical application
For fatwa, these rates form the basis, but the ruler has authority to modify them according to economic conditions. The modern Hanafi view allows adjustment while using these as benchmarks.
The common misunderstanding is that these rates are fixed forever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْخَرَاجُ الَّذِي وَضَعَهُ عُمَرُ بْنُ الْخَطَّابِ - رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ - عَلَى أَهْلِ السَّوَادِ فِي كُلِّ جَرِيبٍ يَبْلُغُهُ الْمَاءُ قَفِيزُهَا شَمَيٌّ وَهُوَ الصَّاعُ وَدِرْهَمُ وَفِي جَرِيبِ الرَّطْبَةِ خَمْسَةُ دَرَاهِمَ وَفِي جَرِيبِ الْكَرْمِ الْمُتَصِل وَالنَّخْلِ الْمُتَّصِلِ عَشَرَةُ دَرَاهِمَ
And the kharaj that Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) imposed on the people of Sawad: for every jarib that is reached by water, its qafiz is shami (it is the sa') and a dirham; and for a jarib of green fodder, five dirhams; and for a jarib of continuous grapevine and continuous palm trees, ten dirhams.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This states the historical kharaj rates set by Caliph Umar on the agricultural land of Sawad (Iraq). For each jarib (a unit of area) of irrigated land growing grains (wheat, barley), the tax was one qafiz (a measure of grain) and one dirham. The text clarifies that 'shami' refers to the sa' (a specific volume). For land growing raṭbah (fresh fodder), the tax was five dirhams per jarib. For vineyards and date palms, provided they are continuous (i.e., densely planted), the tax was ten dirhams per jarib. These rates serve as a precedent for kharaj assessment. The evidence from Umar's practice is cited from the works of Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi, confirming the historical application.

Key Terms
جَرِيب (jarib): A unit of area used for land measurement, approximately 1592 square meters.
قَفِيز (qafiz): A measure of volume for grains.
شَمِي (shami): A term for a measure of grain, explained as equivalent to the sa'.
صَاع (sa'): A measure of volume, approximately 2.6-3.0 liters.
رَطْبَة (raṭbah): Green fodder, fresh grass for animal feed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the kharaj rate per jarib of irrigated wheat according to Umar's settlement?
A) 1 qafiz and 1 dirham
B) 10 dirhams
C) 5 dirhams
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Umar's kharaj rates, recorded in early hadith sources, serve as a binding precedent in the Hanafi school but are not immutable; the ruler may adjust them by istiḥsān.

Kharaj on Other Crops: Taxation According to Capacity
Ruling on kharaj for other crops with capacity-based reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on assessing kharaj for non-staple crops based on land capacity and explain the imam's duty to reduce the tax if the land cannot bear it.

Core Idea
The imam must assess kharaj by land capacity and reduce it when burdensome.

We have seen that kharaj on grains, grapes, and dates is fixed. But what about other crops like vegetables, fruits, or cash crops? The Hanafi school extends the principle of capacity-based taxation to all other agricultural produce, ensuring justice and preventing overburdening. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its evidence from the practice of Caliph Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expansion
The text expands the kharaj system beyond the three staple categories (grains, grapes, dates) to all other varieties of crops. Underline 'wa ma siwa dhalika' in the Arabic.
2
Understand the capacity principle
The key term is 'bi-hasab al-taqah' — according to capacity. This means the tax must be proportionate to the land's productive ability, not a fixed arbitrary amount.
Taqah refers to the land's ability to yield produce after covering the farmer's needs.
3
Note the imam's obligation to reduce
If the imposed kharaj exceeds the land's capacity, the imam must reduce it. This is a duty, not a mere option. The Arabic 'naqasaha al-imam' uses the verb indicating obligation.
This prevents exploitation and ensures the land remains productive.
4
Trace the evidence from Umar
The proof comes from the confirmed practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who instructed his governors not to burden the land beyond its means. The hadith shows Umar's concern and his acceptance of their assessment based on capacity.
Qawl Sahabi is a source of law in Hanafi usul, especially when not contradicted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا سِوَى ذَلِكَ مِنْ الْأَصْنَافِ يُوضَعُ عَلَيْهَا بِحَسَبِ الطَّاقَةِ فَإِنْ لَمْ تُطِقْ مَا وُضِعَ عَلَيْهَا نَقَصَهَا الْإِمَامُ
And whatever other varieties [of crops] besides that, a tax is placed upon them according to capacity; if they cannot bear what was placed upon them, the imam reduces it.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This expands the kharaj system beyond grains, grapes, and dates to all other crops. The imam (ruler) has discretion to assess kharaj based on the land's productive capacity. Moreover, if the fixed tax proves too burdensome and the land cannot yield enough to pay it, the imam must reduce the assessment. This reflects the principle of justice in taxation. The evidence from the practice of Umar is cited: he instructed his officials not to overburden the land, and when asked, they confirmed they had set a rate the land could bear. Also, the report about Uthman's adjustments shows that different crops had different rates. The Hanafi principle here is that kharaj is not arbitrary but tied to the land's capability.

Key Terms
طَاقَة (taqah): The productive capacity of the land to pay the tax; the ability to produce enough surplus after meeting the cultivator's needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the basis for placing kharaj on crops other than grains, grapes, and dates?
A) A fixed rate per acre irrespective of yield
B) The market value of the crop at harvest
C) The land's productive capacity (taqah)
D) The personal discretion of the imam without any condition

Key Takeaway
Kharaj on all crops must be assessed by the land's capacity, and the imam must reduce it if the land cannot bear the burden.

Kharaj Due Despite Damage or Neglect
Ruling on kharaj when land is flooded, crops destroyed, or owner neglects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios that affect kharaj liability on kharaj land and apply the rule to determine when the tax is due.

Core Idea
Natural calamity waives kharaj; owner neglect does not.

Kharaj is a land tax on agricultural land in Islamic governance. But what happens if the land fails to produce? This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling that balances divine intervention and human responsibility: a natural disaster that destroys the crop excuses the tax, but the owner's deliberate neglect does not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of kharaj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the Arabic ruling and note the two distinct cases: (1) natural calamity (flood or blight) destroys the crop, (2) the owner neglects the land by leaving it uncultivated.
The Arabic uses 'ghalaba al-mā' (water overcomes) and 'āfah' (blight) for the first scenario, and 'ʻaṭṭalahā' (neglects it) for the second.
2
Determine the liability for each
For the first scenario: 'fa lā kharāja ʻalayhim' – no kharaj upon them. For the second: 'fa ʻalayhi al-kharāj' – upon him is the kharaj. Note the different pronouns: 'them' (the taxpayers) vs. 'him' (the owner).
The word 'them' refers to the cultivators (who may include tenants), but in the second case it is specifically the owner.
3
Understand the rationale
The first scenario is an unavoidable act of God (āfah samāwiyyah). The second is a result of the owner's own choice. Islamic law does not impose liability when performance is impossible due to external forces, but it holds people accountable for their deliberate inaction.
This aligns with the legal maxim: 'Lā taklīf illā bi al-maqdūr' (No obligation except within capacity).
4
Apply to new cases
Consider analogous situations: if a pest destroys half the crop, does the tax reduce proportionally? If the owner plants but fails to irrigate, is that neglect? Use the principle: natural cause = waiver, human cause = no waiver.
Hanafi scholars may differ on partial damage; this ruling addresses total destruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ غَلَبَ عَلَى أَرْضِ الْخَرَاجِ الْمَاءُ وَانْقَطَعَ عَنْهَا أَوْ اصْطَلَمَ الزَّرْعَ آفَةٌ فَلَا خَرَاجَ عَلَيْهِمْ وَإِنْ عَطَّلَهَا صَاحِبُهَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْخَرَاجُ
And if water overcomes the kharaj land and is cut off from it, or a blight severs the crop, then there is no kharaj upon them; and if its owner neglects it, then upon him is the kharaj.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This ruling addresses two scenarios. First, if kharaj land becomes flooded (waterlogging) and irrigation is cut off, or if the crop is destroyed by a natural blight (like locusts, frost, etc.), then the kharaj tax is waived for that year. This is because the land failed to produce due to an unavoidable calamity. Second, if the owner himself neglects the land (does not cultivate it without valid excuse), the kharaj remains obligatory. The rationale is that the owner's deliberate inaction does not excuse the tax; the land is still capable, but the owner chose not to use it. In contrast, natural disasters beyond human control excuse the tax. This balance reflects Hanafi principles of fairness.

Key Terms
آفَة (afah): A blight or natural calamity that destroys the crop.
عَطَّلَ (attala): To leave land uncultivated without a valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling on kharaj, which of the following excuses the payment of kharaj?
A) The owner goes on pilgrimage and leaves the land fallow
B) A flood destroys the entire crop
C) The owner decides to switch to a non-agricultural use
D) The market price of crops collapses

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is waived only when crop failure is caused by a natural calamity beyond the owner's control; deliberate neglect does not excuse the tax.

Kharaj on Land of a Muslim Convert or Buyer
Rulings on kharaj when owner converts to Islam or Muslim buys kharaj land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kharaj when a non-Muslim owner converts to Islam or when a Muslim buys kharaj land, and explain the evidence from the Companions that kharaj is a right of the land.

Core Idea
Kharaj is a right of the land, not the person; it remains after conversion or sale.

The Hudud (punishments) are a set of fixed penalties prescribed by the Shari'ah for major crimes. In this lesson, we examine the ruling on kharaj (land tax) when the owner of kharaj land converts to Islam, and when a Muslim purchases such land from a non-Muslim. The Hanafi school holds that kharaj is a right attached to the land itself, so it survives changes in the owner's religion or identity. This position is supported by the practice of the Caliphs Umar and Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two situations: (1) a non-Muslim owner of kharaj land converts to Islam, and (2) a Muslim buys kharaj land from a dhimmi. In each, the ruling is that kharaj is still taken.
Conversion or purchase does not affect the land's obligation.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For the convert: kharaj continues 'as before' (على حاله). For the buyer: kharaj is taken from the Muslim buyer. The ruling is that kharaj is mandatory on all owners of kharaj land, regardless of their personal status.
This distinguishes kharaj from jizyah, which is personal and canceled upon conversion.
3
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi position relies on the statement (qawl) of Umar and Ali: 'If he converts to Islam and has land, we remove the jizyah from him and take its kharaj.' This indicates that kharaj remains.
Source: Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit distinction between jizyah (removed) and kharaj (taken) shows that kharaj is a separate obligation tied to the land, not to the person's religion. The imperative 'we take' (أخذنا) indicates continuity.
Thubut: zanni (narrated by one chain), but dalala: qati (clear meaning).
5
Note the ikhtilaf and Hanafi response
Some scholars, like Ibn Umar, disliked the practice, but the Hanafi school permits it. The evidence from the Caliphs and the land-right principle outweighs the personal dislike.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih, but the Hanafi mufta bihi is permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَسْلَمَ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْخَرَاجِ أُخِذَ مِنْهُ الْخَرَاجُ عَلَى حَالِهِ ١٢ وَيَجُوزُ أَنْ يَشْتَرِيَ الْمُسْلِمُ أَرْضَ الْخَرَاجِ مِنْ اللَّهِيَ وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الْخَرَاجُ
And whoever from the people of kharaj becomes Muslim, kharaj is taken from him as before. And it is permissible for a Muslim to buy kharaj land from the dhimmi, and kharaj is taken from him.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
Two important rulings: First, if a non-Muslim landowner (dhimmi) who owns kharaj land converts to Islam, the kharaj tax continues to be levied on the land; it is not canceled due to his conversion. This is because kharaj is a right attached to the land itself, not a personal tax (jizyah is personal and canceled upon conversion). Evidence from Umar and Ali is cited: they said that upon conversion, jizyah is removed but kharaj remains. Second, a Muslim may purchase kharaj land from a non-Muslim, and the kharaj remains obligatory on the Muslim buyer. The evidence includes reports that Hasan and Husayn bought kharaj land and paid kharaj, and also a hadith from Nafi' where Ibn Umar discouraged it but it is still permitted. The Hanafi position is that kharaj is a land tax that survives transfer of ownership and changes in religion.

Key Terms
ذِمِّي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a covenant.
جِزْيَة (jizyah): Personal poll tax on non-Muslims; canceled upon conversion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi who owns kharaj land converts to Islam, what happens to his tax obligations?
A) Both jizyah and kharaj are canceled
B) Jizyah is canceled, kharaj remains
C) Kharaj is canceled, jizyah remains
D) Both remain

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is a right of the land itself; it continues after conversion or sale, and the Muslim buyer must pay it.

No 'Ushr on Produce from Kharaj Land
Ruling with proof from al-Sha'bi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that produce from kharaj land is exempt from 'ushr and cite the evidence from al-Sha'bi.

Core Idea
Kharaj and 'ushr do not combine on the same land; once kharaj is levied, 'ushr is dropped.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Hanafi fiscal law: the non-combination of kharaj and 'ushr. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying agricultural taxes in Islamic jurisprudence. The ruling is straightforward but rests on a sound legal basis.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify what the text rules: that there is no 'ushr on produce from kharaj land.
The ruling is a negative obligation – 'ushr is not due.
2
Recall the Evidence
The text cites the statement of al-Sha'bi: 'Kharaj and 'ushr do not combine on the same land.' Recognize that this is a saying of a Tabi'i and considered a valid proof in Hanafi fiqh.
The evidence is transmitted in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
3
Understand the Reasoning
Kharaj is a fixed annual land tax, while 'ushr is proportional to each harvest. Since kharaj land is already subject to an annual tax, imposing 'ushr would be double taxation, which is prohibited.
The principle prevents unfair burden on the cultivator.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a farmer who owns kharaj land and harvests a crop. According to the ruling, he only pays the fixed kharaj, not 'ushr. Conversely, if he owns ushri land, he pays 'ushr per harvest.
Land classification is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا عُشْرَ فِي الْخَارِجَ مِنْ أَرْضِ الْخَرَاجِ
And there is no 'ushr on what comes out of kharaj land.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This ruling establishes that produce grown on kharaj land is not subject to 'ushr (tithe). The two taxes, kharaj and 'ushr, do not combine on the same land. Kharaj is a fixed land tax, while 'ushr is a proportional tax on agricultural produce. Once kharaj is levied, 'ushr is not imposed on the same produce. Evidence from al-Sha'bi is cited: 'kharaj and 'ushr do not combine on the same land.' The usul (principle) mentioned at the end of the passage explains that kharaj is imposed once per year, while 'ushr is based on the crop itself and is due each time a crop is harvested. However, since kharaj land is already subject to the fixed annual tax, 'ushr is not additionally levied. This prevents double taxation.

Key Terms
عُشْر (ushr): A tithe of 10% or 5% on agricultural produce from ushri land.
خَرَاج (kharaj): A fixed land tax on kharaji land, regardless of produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can the same land be subject to both kharaj and 'ushr according to Hanafi law?
A) Yes, if it is irrigated
B) No, they do not combine
C) Only if the owner is Muslim
D) Only if the land is conquered by force

Key Takeaway
Kharaj land is exempt from 'ushr because the two taxes cannot coexist on the same land.

Permissible Expenditure of Jizyah Funds
States permissible uses of jizyah funds for public welfare.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the categories of permissible jizyah expenditure according to the Hanafi school and explain why they are restricted to Muslim public welfare.

Core Idea
Jizyah is a public trust: it must be spent on Muslim welfare, not personal gain.

Jizyah is not a personal tax for the ruler but a public fund designated for the well-being of the Muslim community. This passage from al-Quduri specifies exactly how it may be spent, ensuring transparency and accountability. Understanding these categories helps you grasp the Hanafi vision of a state treasury bound by shar‘i guidelines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Recognize that jizyah is earmarked for ‘masalih al-muslimin’ (public welfare of Muslims). This is the overarching rule that governs all expenditures.
‘Masalih’ is plural of ‘maslahah’ – benefit or interest.
2
Memorize the categories
List the seven (or eight) categories mentioned: fortification of frontiers, bridges/causeways, judges’ salaries, officials’ salaries, scholars’ provision, fighters’ stipends, and dependents’ support.
The Arabic terms: taghur, qanatir, jussur, qudah, ummah, ulama, muqatilah, dharari.
3
Apply the restriction
Understand that any use outside these categories is impermissible. This keeps jizyah from being misused for private or non‑beneficial projects.
Other schools allow wider discretion; Hanafis insist on these specific heads.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْجُزْيَةِ تُصْرَفُ فِي مَصَالِحِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ فَيُسَدُّ بِهِ التَّغُورُ وَتُبْنَى بِهِ الْقَنَاطِرُ وَالْجُسُورُ وَيُعْطَى قُضَاةُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَعُمَّاهُمْ وَعُلَمَاؤُهُمْ مِنْهَا مَا يَكْفِيهِمْ وَيُدْفَعُ مِنْهُ أَرْزَاقُ الْمُقَاتِلَةِ وَذَرَارِتِهِمْ
And jizyah is spent for the welfare of the Muslims: with it the frontier posts are fortified, bridges and causeways are built, the judges of the Muslims, their officials, and their scholars are given from it what suffices them, and from it are paid the maintenance of the fighters and their dependents.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Quduri. It specifies that jizyah—the poll tax collected from non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis)—is not a personal levy but a public fund designated for Muslim welfare interests (masalih al-muslimin). The passage enumerates eight permissible categories: (1) fortification of frontier posts, (2) building bridges and causeways, (3) salaries for judges, (4) salaries for government officials, (5) provision for scholars, (6) stipends for fighters, and (7) support for their dependents. The ruling implies that jizyah cannot be used for personal gain or projects that do not serve the Muslim community. The mention of qanatir (bridges) and jussur (causeways) indicates that infrastructure benefiting the public is included.

Key Terms
الْجُزْيَةِ (al-jizyah): The annual poll tax levied on free non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
مَصَالِحِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ (masalih al-muslimin): Public welfare interests of the Muslim community, including defense, infrastructure, and administration.
التَّغُورُ (al-taghur): Frontier posts or border fortresses that require maintenance and garrisoning.
الْمُقَاتِلَةِ (al-muqatilah): The fighting men (combatants) eligible for stipends from the treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one permissible use of jizyah funds?
A) Personal wealth of the caliph
B) Building bridges and causeways
C) Gifts to non-Muslim dignitaries
D) Funding foreign wars beyond defensive needs

Key Takeaway
Jizyah funds are restricted to eight specific welfare categories: frontiers, infrastructure, judiciary, administration, scholars, military, and their dependents.

Rulings on Fighting Muslim Rebels (Bughat)
Hanafi ruling on the treatment of Muslim rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for fighting Muslim rebels and distinguish the rules based on whether the rebels have a fia.

Core Idea
The Imam must seek reconciliation first, and treatment of rebels depends on their organization, not mere rebellion.

This lesson presents the Hanafi framework for dealing with Muslim rebels (bughat). The passage from al-Quduri outlines the steps the Imam must take before any military action, and the nuanced rules that apply during and after conflict. Crucially, the distinction between a rebel group with a stable force (fia) and one without determines whether harsher measures are permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-conflict obligations
The Imam must first call the rebels to return to unity and clarify their doubts. Fighting is not initiated until the rebels strike first.
This reflects the principle of reconciliation.
2
Examine the rules of engagement
Once rebels initiate, fighting is limited to breaking their assembly. The goal is not annihilation but restoring order.
The phrase 'حَتَّى نُفَرِّقَ جَمْعَهُمْ' indicates the objective.
3
Differentiate based on fia
If the rebels have an organized force (fia), wounded may be dispatched and retreating pursued. Without fia, these are prohibited.
Fia indicates a persistent threat; absence suggests the rebellion is not sustained.
4
Understand property and family protections
Rebels' children are not enslaved, and their property is not distributed. The Imam holds the property until genuine repentance.
Property is returned only after tawbah, not merely surrender.
5
Permissibility of using rebel weapons
If Muslims need them, they may use the rebels' own weapons against them. This indicates captured arms are not immediately distributed.
This is a practical exception for necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: وَإِذَا تَغَلَّبَ قَوْمٌ مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ عَلَى بَلَدٍ وَخَرَجُوا عَنْ طَاعَةِ الْإِمَامِ دَعَاهُمْ إِلَى الْعَوْدِ إِلَى جَمَاعَتِهِمْ وَكَشَفَ عَنْ شُبْهَتِهِمْ وَلَا يَبْدَؤُهُمْ بِقِتَالٍ حَتَّى يَبْدَءُوهُ فَإِنْ بَدَءُونَا قَاتَلْنَاهُمْ حَتَّى نُفَرِّقَ جَمْعَهُمْ فَإِنْ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ فِئَةٌ أُجْهِزَ عَلَى جَرِيحِهِمْ وَاتُّبِعَ مُوَلِّيهِمْ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُمْ فِئَةٌ لَمْ يُجْهَزْ عَلَى جَرِيحِهِمْ وَلَمْ يُتْبَعْ مُوَلِّيهِمْ وَلَا تُسْبَى مِنْهُمْ ذُرِّيَّةٌ وَلَا يُقَسَّمُ مِنْهُمْ مَالٌ وَلَا بَأْسَ أَنْ يُقَاتَلُوا بِسِلَاحِهِمْ إِنِ احْتَاجَ الْمُسْلِمُونَ إِلَيْهِ وَيَحْبِسُ الْإِمَامُ أَمْوَالَهُمْ وَلَا يَرُدُّهَا عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا يُقَسِّمُهَا حَتَّى يَتُوبُوا فَيَرُدَّهَا عَلَيْهِمْ
Section: When a group of Muslims gains control of a territory and rebels against the Imam's authority, he calls them to return to unity and clarifies their doubts. He does not initiate fighting against them until they initiate fighting. If they start fighting us, we fight them until we break their assembly. If they have a [reliable] force, their wounded are dispatched (given finishing blow) and their retreating ones are pursued. But if they have no [reliable] force, their wounded are not dispatched and their retreating ones are not pursued. Their children are not taken captive and their property is not distributed. There is no harm in fighting them with their own weapons if the Muslims need them. The Imam holds (withholds) their property—he does not return it to them and does not distribute it until they repent, then he returns it to them.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the matn of al-Quduri concerning the treatment of baghi (rebel) groups that arise within the Muslim community. The Hanafi school provides a distinct legal framework: the Imam must first seek reconciliation by calling the rebels back to obedience and clarifying their doubts (shubahah). Only if the rebels initiate armed conflict is fighting permissible, and even then, the goal is to break their cohesion, not annihilate them. The text draws a critical distinction based on whether the rebels have a 'fia'—a stable, organized force. If they do, stronger measures (executing wounded, pursuing fugitives) are allowed; if not, leniency is applied: wounded are left, fugitives are not chased. Furthermore, property and children of rebels are protected: no enslavement, no confiscation or distribution unless repentance occurs. The Imam may retain their property as a security measure, returning it upon repentance. The source also permits using the rebels' own captured weapons against them if needed, indicating that the weapons are considered booty but not immediately distributed. This ruling is based on the practice of Caliph Ali with the Khawarij, as cited in the proofs.

Key Terms
الْبَغْي (al-baghy): Rebellion against the legitimate Imam by a group with a justifying interpretation (ta'wil).
فِئَة (fi'ah): A group (force) that serves as a rallying point for the rebels; indicates persistent rebellion.
التَّوْبَة (al-tawbah): Repentance of the rebels, signified by returning to obedience and abandoning their rebellion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what must the Imam do before fighting rebels?
A) Immediately attack to suppress the rebellion
B) Call them to return and clarify their doubts
C) Seize their property and families
D) Seek permission from other scholars

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi law of rebellion prioritizes reconciliation and proportionality, with property held in trust until repentance.

Wearing Silk Brocade in War: Sahibayn vs. Abu Hanifa
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on wearing silk brocade in war, explain the evidence for each view, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Silk in war is permissible by concession; Abu Hanifa dislikes it, but the Sahibayn allow it.

This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: may a man wear silk brocade (deebaj) during battle? Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani see no harm, citing prophetic concessions, while Imam Abu Hanifa considers it disliked. Understanding the evidence each side invokes reveals how the Hanafi school balances general prohibitions against specific necessitating circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (wearing silk brocade in war) and what each Hanafi authority says: Abu Hanifa says makruh, Sahibayn say la ba'sa (no harm).
The matn states the ruling before giving evidence.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's evidence
Examine the two hadith from Anas and the statement of Ata al-Tabi'i. Note how they use the prophetic concession (rukhsa) for lice in battle as a proof for general permissibility in war.
The hadith are ahad, so they are zanni al-thubut.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's basis
Although no direct evidence is cited for Abu Hanifa, infer his position from the general prohibition of silk for men and the statement of Ikrimah who disliked it even in war, considering it inappropriate for martyrdom.
Abu Hanifa may also consider the hadith limited to the specific case of lice.
4
Locate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, wearing silk brocade in war is makruh tanzihi (rewardable to avoid); under the Sahibayn, it is permissible and no sin.
The mufta bihi view often follows the Sahibayn due to the explicit hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بَأْسَ بِلُبْسِ الدِّيباج عِنْدَهُمَا فِي الْحَرْبِ وَيُكْرَهُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ .
وجه (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الديباج عندهما في الحرب 
عَنْ عَطَاءِ، قَالَ: «لَا بَأْسَ بِلُبْسِ الْحَرِيرِ فِي الْحَرْبِ».
وجه (۲) الحديث لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الديباج عندهما في الحرب 
عَنْ أَنَسٍ: «أَنَّ عَبْدَ الرَّحْمَنِ بْنَ عَوْفٍ وَالزُّبَيْرَ شَكَوَا إِلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَعْنِي الْقَمْلَ فَأَرْخَصَ لَهُمَا فِي الْحَرِيرِ فَرَأَيْتُهُ عَلَيْهِمَا فِي غَزَاةٍ».
وجه (۳) الحديث لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الديباج عندهما في الحرب 
عَنْ أَنَسِ بْنِ مَالِكٍ: «أَنَّ عَبْدَ الرَّحْمَنِ بْنَ عَوْفٍ، وَالزُّبَيْرَ بْنَ الْعَوَّامِ، شَكَوَا الْقَمْلَ إِلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فِي غَزَاةٍ فَرَخَّصَ لَهُمَا فِي قُمُصِ الْحَرِيرِ».
وجه (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الديباج عندهما في الحرب 
عَنْ عِكْرِمَةَ، أَنَّهُ كَرِهَهُ فِي الْحَرْبِ، وَقَالَ: «أَرْجَى مَا يَكُونُ لِلشَّهَادَةِ».
And there is no harm in wearing silk brocade according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in war, and it is disliked according to Abu Hanifa. Evidence (1) saying of a tabi'i to establish: There is no harm in wearing silk brocade according to them in war: from Ata who said: There is no harm in wearing silk in war. Evidence (2) the hadith to establish: ... from Anas that Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and al-Zubayr complained to the Prophet (about lice) and he granted them a concession for silk, and I saw them wearing it in a battle. Evidence (3) the hadith... Evidence (1) saying of a Companion to establish: ... from Ikrimah that he disliked it in war and said: 'It is the most hopeful time for martyrdom.'
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage addresses wearing silk brocade (deebaj) during war. The matn states that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) see no harm in it, while Abu Hanifa considers it disliked (makruh). The commentator then provides evidence. The first is a statement of the tabi'i Ata ibn Abi Rabah, who explicitly permitted silk in war. The second and third are hadith from Anas that the Prophet allowed Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and al-Zubayr to wear silk shirts during a battle due to lice. This indicates a concession (rukhsa) for necessity. The fourth evidence is from Ikrimah, who disliked it in war, despite the concession. This seems to be cited as a counter-opinion, but the commentator includes it to show the range of views. The Hanafi position is that silk is inherently haram for men, but necessity in war may allow it. The Sahibayn focus on the specific concession, while Abu Hanifa hesitates because war is a time of expected martyrdom and luxury is inappropriate.

Key Terms
الديباج (al-deebaj): A type of silk brocade, often woven with gold or silver threads.
الحرب (al-harb): War, battle, or combat situation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'illa (effective cause) that the Sahibayn use to generalize the prophetic concession for silk in war?
A) The specific mention of lice
B) The need (hajah) or necessity in battle
C) The high status of the Companions involved
D) The fact that the battle was defensive

Key Takeaway
Wearing silk in war is a ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa dislikes it, the Sahibayn permit it, and the latter is the practical fatwa due to the clear hadith.

Ruling on Wearing Mixed Silk Fabric (Mulham)
Ruling on wearing mixed fabric with silk warp and cotton/kazz weft: permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wearing mulham and identify the evidence from the actions of the Companions.

Core Idea
Mixed silk fabric (mulham) is permissible to wear — only pure silk is prohibited for men.

This lesson addresses the ruling on a specific type of mixed fabric called mulham, which has a silk warp and a cotton or khazz weft. The text states there is no harm in wearing it, and then provides three pieces of evidence from the Companions' practice. Understanding this ruling clarifies that not all silk-containing fabrics are impermissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wearing a garment made of mixed fabric (mulham) where the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz. The ruling is 'la ba'sa' (no harm), meaning permissible (mubah).
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The commentator provides three pieces of evidence: a report from Sa'd that the Prophet gave him a khazz turban, a statement from Abu Dawud that twenty or more Companions wore khazz, and a report that Abu Bakrah wore a khazz cloak with silk warp.
All evidence comes from actions of Sahaba or the Prophet's approval.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The actions of the Companions, known to the Prophet and not rebuked, indicate permissibility. Abu Dawud's report implies consensus (ijma' fi'li) among numerous Sahaba.
This is strong evidence for the Hanafi school.
4
Note any ikhtilaf
There is no significant dispute within the Hanafi school or across madhhabs regarding the permissibility of mulham. The ruling is agreed upon.
Some schools may differ on pure silk, but here mixed fabric is allowed.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is that wearing mulham (silk warp, cotton/khazz weft) is permissible. No harm in wearing it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بَأْسَ بِلُبْسِ الْمُلْحَمِ الْحَرِيرِ إِذَا كَانَ سُدَاهُ إِبْرَيْسَمًا وَحُمَتُهُ قُطْنَا أَوْ خَزَّا.
وجه (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الملحم الحرير إذا كان سداه 
أَخْبَرَنِي أَبِي عَبْدُ اللَّهِ بْنُ سَعْدٍ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ سَعْدٍ، قَالَ: رَأَيْتُ رَجُلًا بِبُخَارَى عَلَى بَغْلَةٍ بَيْضَاءَ عَلَيْهِ عِمَامَةُ خَزٍّ سَوْدَاءُ، فَقَالَ: «كَسَانِيهَا رَسُولُ اللهِ».
وجه (۲) الحديث لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الملحم الحرير إذا كان سداه 
قَالَ أَبُو دَاوُدَ: «وَعِشْرُونَ نَفْسًا مِنْ أَصْحَابِ رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم أَوْ أَكْثَرُ لَبِسُوا الْخَزَّ مِنْهُمْ أَنَسَ، وَالْبَرَاءُ بْنُ عَازِبٍ».
وجه (۳) الحديث لثبوت ولا بأس بلبس الملحم الحرير 
عَنْ أَبِيهِ، قَالَ: «كَانَ لِأَبِي بَكْرَةَ مِطْرَفُ خَزٍّ سُدَاهُ حَرِيرٌ، وَكَانَ يَلْبَسُهُ».
لغات: سُدَاهُ: تانا، وَحَمَتُهُ: بانا، إِبْرَيْسَمًا: ريشم، قُطْنَا: روئی۔
And there is no harm in wearing mixed silk fabric (mulham) when its warp is silk thread and its weft is cotton or khazz. Evidence (1) saying of a Companion to establish there is no harm in wearing mulham when its warp is... My father Abdullah ibn Sa'd informed me from his father Sa'd said: I saw a man in Bukhara on a white mule wearing a black turban of khazz, so he said: The Messenger of Allah clothed me with it. Evidence (2) the hadith to establish... Abu Dawud said: Twenty or more Companions of the Messenger of Allah wore khazz, among them Anas and al-Bara ibn Azib. Evidence (3) the hadith to establish... From his father he said: Abu Bakrah had a cloak of khazz whose warp was silk, and he used to wear it. Vocabulary: sudahu: warp, hamatuhu: weft, ibrisaman: silk, qutnan: cotton.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage concerns mulham, a fabric where the warp (vertical threads) is silk and the weft (horizontal threads) is cotton or khazz (a mixed fabric). The matn states there is no harm (la ba'sa) in wearing it. The commentator provides three pieces of evidence. The first is a report from Sa'd that the Prophet gave him a turban of khazz. The second is a statement by Abu Dawud that many Companions wore khazz. The third is that Abu Bakrah had a cloak of khazz with silk warp and he wore it. These show that wearing mixed silk fabric was common among the early Muslims. The vocabulary section clarifies the terms: sudah = warp, hama(tu) = weft, ibrisam = silk, qutn = cotton. The Hanafi ruling is that if silk is only part of the fabric, it is permissible as long as it is not predominantly silk or used for adornment.

Key Terms
الملحم (al-mulham): A fabric with silk warp and cotton or other material weft.
الخز (al-khazz): A mixed fabric, often part silk and part wool or cotton.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on wearing a garment whose warp is silk and weft is cotton?
A) Haram for men
B) Makruh
C) Permissible (no harm)
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
Wearing mulham (mixed silk warp and cotton weft) is permissible, as proven by the practice of the Companions.

Looking at Non-Mahram Women: Face and Hands Only
Ruling and proofs on permissibility of looking at non-mahram woman's face and hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a non-mahram woman, identify the condition of desire, and explain the Quranic and hadith evidences for the exception.

Core Idea
Looking at a non-mahram woman is haram except face and hands; if desire is feared, even that is prohibited.

The passage establishes the baseline prohibition of looking at non-mahram women, with the exception of the face and hands. However, this exception is conditional: if a man fears sexual desire, even the face becomes prohibited unless there is a genuine need. The text then cites four proofs from the Quran and Sunnah to support this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Exception
State the general ruling: looking at a non-mahram woman is not permissible except at her face and hands. Note the condition: if desire is feared, even the face is not allowed except for need.
The exception is only for the face and hands; all other body parts are prohibited unconditionally.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The first proof from Quran 24:30 commands men to lower their gaze. This implies a general prohibition, but does not specify exceptions. The exception is derived from other texts.
The imperative 'yaghuddu' indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine the Exception in the Quran
Quran 24:31 commands women not to display adornment except 'what appears thereof'. The Companions, especially Ibn Abbas, interpreted this as the face and hands, establishing the exception.
The phrase 'illa ma zahara' is general ('amm) and must be qualified by the Sunnah.
4
Confirm with Sunnah
The hadith of Asma (Sunan Abi Dawud) where the Prophet explicitly indicated face and hands, and the tafsir of Ibn Abbas, confirm that only these parts are permissible to view.
These ahadith are ahad but are considered sufficient for establishing the ruling due to their clarity.
5
Consider the Condition of Desire
The permissibility of looking at face and hands is contingent upon safety from desire. If a man fears desire (shahwah), it becomes haram to look even at the face, except for a genuine need such as medical treatment or giving testimony.
This condition shows that the ruling is not absolute; it varies based on the individual's state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنظُرَ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الْأَجْنَبِيَّةِ إلَّا إلَى وَجْهِهَا وَكَفَّيْه فَإِنْ كَانَ لَا يَأْمَنُ الشَّهْوَةَ لَا يَنْظُرُإِلَى وَجْهِهَا إِلَّا لِحَاجَةٍ

وجه: (1) الآية لثبوت وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الْأَجْنَبِيَّةِ \ ﴿قُل لِّلْمُؤْمِنِينَ يَغُضُّوا مِنْ أَبْصَرِهِمْ وَيَحْفَظُوا فُرُوجَهُمْ ذَلِكَ أَزْكَى لَهُمْ إِنَّ اللَّهَ خَبِيرٌ بِمَا يَصْنَعُونَ﴾ (سورة النور، 24 أيت، نمبر 30)
وجه:(۲) الآية لثبوت وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الْأَجْنَبِيَّةِ \ وَقُل لِّلْمُؤْمِنَاتِ يَغْضُضْنَ مِنْ أَبْصَرِهِنَّ وَيَحْفَظْنَ فُرُوجَهُنَّ وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا وَلْيَضْرِبْنَ بِخُمُرِهِنَّ عَلَى جُيُوبِهِنَّ وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا لِبُعُولَتِهِنَّ ) (سورة النور ، 24 أيت، نمبر 31)
وجه : (۳) الحديث لثبوت وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الْأَجْنَبِيَّةِ \ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: {وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا} [النور: 31] قَالَ: "مَا فِي الْكَفِّ وَالْوَجْهِ "السنن الكبري للبيهقي، بَابُ عَوْرَةِ الْمَرْأَةِ الحَرَّةِ، غير 3214)
وجه : (٤) الحديث لثبوت وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الْأَجْنَبِيَّةِ \ عَنْ عَائِشَةَ ، أَنَّ أَسْمَاءَ بنتَ أَبي بَكْرٍ، دَخَلَتْ عَلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَعَلَيْهَا ثِيَابٌ رِقَاقٌ، فَأَعْرَضَ عَنْهَا رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَقَالَ: «يَا أَسْمَاءُ، إِنَّ الْمَرْأَةَ إِذَا بَلَغَتِ الْمَحِيضَ لَمْ تَصْلُحْ أَنْ يُرَى مِنْهَا إِلَّا هَذَا وَهَذَا» وَأَشَارَ إِلَى وَجْهِهِ وَكَفَّيْهِ ، سنن ابوداود، بَابٌ فِيمَا تُبْدِي الْمَرْأَةُ مِنْ زِينَتِهَا نمبر 4104)
اصول : عورت عام حالات میں اپنی زینت کی جگہ مرد کے سامنے ظاہر نہ کرے، بلکہ مرد نگاہیں نیچی رکھے اور عورت بھی نگاہ نیچی رکھے تاکہ برائی کا راستہ ہموار نہ ہو۔
And it is not permissible for a man to look at a non-mahram woman except at her face and her two hands. If he does not feel safe from desire, he should not look at her face except for a need. Evidence: (1) The verse for establishing: 'Say to the believing men to lower their eyes and guard their private parts; that is purer for them. Indeed Allah is Aware of what they do.' (Surah al-Nur, 24:30). Evidence: (2) The verse for establishing: 'And say to the believing women to lower their eyes and guard their private parts and not display their adornment except what appears thereof...' (Surah al-Nur, 24:31). Evidence: (3) The hadith for establishing: from Ibn Abbas who said about {and not display their adornment except what appears thereof} [al-Nur 24:31]: 'What is on the hands and the face.' (Sunan al-Kubra lil-Bayhaqi, Chapter on the Awrah of a Free Woman, no. 3214). Evidence: (4) The hadith for establishing: from Aisha that Asma bint Abi Bakr entered upon the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, wearing thin clothes. The Messenger of Allah turned away from her and said: 'O Asma, when a woman reaches the age of menstruation, it is not appropriate for anything to be seen of her except this and this,' and he pointed to his face and his hands. (Sunan Abi Dawud, Chapter on What a Woman May Display of Her Adornment, no. 4104). Principle: The woman should not generally display her adornment before a man; rather, the man should lower his gaze and the woman should also lower her gaze so that the path to evil is not paved.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a man's looking at a non-mahram woman. The baseline ruling is prohibition (la yajuz) except for the face and hands. However, if the man fears desire (la ya'man al-shahwa), then even the face is prohibited except for a genuine need. Four proofs are given: (1) The command for men to lower their gaze (Surah al-Nur 24:30) implies restriction. (2) The command for women not to display adornment except what appears (24:31) — 'what appears' is interpreted by Ibn Abbas as face and hands. (3) Confirmation from a hadith of Ibn Abbas specifying face and hands. (4) The hadith of Asma where the Prophet indicated that only face and hands are permissible to be seen. The Urdu principle adds that both men and women should lower their gaze to prevent evil.

Key Terms
أجنبية (ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not a mahram (unmarriageable kin) to the man.
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; if a man fears he will be aroused by looking, it becomes haram even at the face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If she is wearing a hijab
B) If he does not feel safe from desire
C) If she is old
D) If it is daytime

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits looking at a non-mahram woman's face and hands only when safe from desire; otherwise, even the face is prohibited except for genuine need.

Physician Looking at Site of Illness: Necessity Exception
Exception for doctor to look at woman's affected body part for treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a physician looking at a woman's awrah for treatment and explain its basis in the Quranic principle of necessity (darurah).

Core Idea
A doctor may look only at the actual site of illness—necessity permits what is otherwise forbidden, but no more.

The Hanafi school, based on the Quranic principle of necessity, makes a clear exception to the rules of awrah. When medical treatment requires it, a physician may look at the specific affected area. The proof is derived from the verse on emergency food prohibitions, extended through qiyas. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and its hukm level. The act is a physician looking at a woman's awrah for medical treatment. The ruling is mubah (permissible) due to necessity.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text. The passage cites Quran al-An'am 6:145, which permits eating forbidden food in dire necessity. This establishes the general principle of darurah.
The verse is dalil naqli (textual evidence) with qati thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the dalil proves the ruling. The principle of necessity (idtirar) is general; by qiyas, looking at awrah for treatment is analogized to eating forbidden food. The illa is darurah. The ruling is restricted to the site of illness only.
The qiyas is from food prohibitions to awrah prohibitions under the common cause of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجُوزُ لِلطَّبِيبِ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَى مَوْضِعِ الْمَرَضِ مِنْهَا

٢٠ وجه: (1) الآية لثبوت وَيَجُوزُ لِلطَّبِيبِ أَنْ يَنْظُرَاقُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمًا عَلَى طَاعِهِ يَطْعَمُهُ إِلَّا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا أَوْ لَحْمَ خِنزِيرٍ فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ أَوْ فِسْقًا أُهِلَّ لِغَيْرِ اللَّهِ بِهِ، فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ غَيْرَ بَاغٍ وَلَاعاده (سورة الانعام، 6 أيت، نمبر 145)
And it is permissible for the physician to look at the place of illness on her. Evidence: (1) The verse for establishing: 'Say, I do not find within that which was revealed to me [anything] forbidden to one who would eat it unless it be a dead animal or blood spilled out or the flesh of swine - for indeed, it is impure - or it be [meat of] an animal dedicated to other than Allah. But whoever is forced [by necessity], neither desiring [it] nor transgressing [its limit]...' (Surah al-An'am, 6:145).
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage provides an exception for a physician to look at the specific site of illness on a woman, even if it is normally prohibited. The proof is the verse in Surah al-An'am (6:145) which permits eating forbidden things in dire necessity. By analogy (qiyas), looking at awrah for medical treatment is permitted under the principle of necessity (darurah). The doctor may only look at the affected area.

Key Terms
طبيب (ṭabīb): A physician who needs to see the site of illness for diagnosis or treatment.
موضع المرض (mawḍiʿ al-maraḍ): The specific area of the body that is ill; only that part may be looked at.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire body
B) Only the face and hands
C) The specific site of illness
D) The upper body only

Key Takeaway
A physician may look at a woman's awrah only at the specific site of illness, based on the Quranic principle of necessity (darurah).

Men Looking at Men: Permissible Except Awrah
Ruling on men looking at other men's bodies: all except navel to knee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man looking at another man's body, identify the awrah limits, and cite the hadith proofs.

Core Idea
A man's awrah for another man is from navel to knee; all else is permissible to look at.

In this lesson, we examine the fiqh ruling on a man looking at another man's body. The Hanafi school, based on explicit hadith, permits looking at all body parts except the zone between the navel and the knee. This area is considered awrah and must be covered. We will analyze the proofs that establish this boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a man looking at another man's body. The ruling: it is permissible to look at the entire body except the area between the navel and the knee, which is haram to look at.
This is the default ruling; no additional conditions are mentioned in the passage.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Two ahadith are cited from Sunan Abi Dawud. The first commands separation of children in beds, implying prohibition of looking at awrah. The second explicitly prohibits looking at what is below the navel and above the knee.
Both are ahad (single-chain) narrations, thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal inference)
From the first hadith, the command to separate implies that seeing the awrah is prohibited. From the second, the explicit prohibition defines the awrah limits. Together they establish that only the area between navel and knee is prohibited; all else is permissible.
The second hadith is stronger in dalalah (clearer meaning) because it directly specifies the limits.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
The ruling is muttafaq alayh among the four Sunni schools; all agree that a man's awrah for another man is from navel to knee, though there are minor differences about the exact boundaries (e.g., whether the navel itself is included).
Within Hanafi, the standard position is that the awrah begins below the navel and ends below the knee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الرَّجُلِ جَمِيعَ بَدَنِهِ إِلَّا مَا بَيْنَ سُرَّتِهِ وَرَكْبَتِهِ

وجه : (۱) الحديث لثبوت وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الرَّجُلِ جَمِيعَ بَدَنِهِ \ عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ، عَنْ أبِيهِ، عَنْ جَدِهِ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مُرُوا أَوْلادَكُمْ بِالصَّلَاةِ وَهُمْ أَبْنَاءُ سَبْعِ سِنِينَ، وَاضْرِبُوهُمْ عَلَيْهَا، وَهُمْ أَبْنَاءُ عَشْرِ وَفَرّقُوا بَيْنَهُمْ فِي الْمَضَاجِعِ » (سنن ابوداود، بَابُ مَتَى يُؤْمَرُ الْغُلَامُ بالصَّلَاةِ، غمبر 495)
وجه:(۲) الحديث لثبوت وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الرَّجُلِ جَمِيعَ بَدَنِهِ \ حَدَّثَنِي دَاوُدُ بْنُ سَوَّارٍ الْمُزَيُّ، بِإِسْنَادِهِ وَمَعْنَاهُ وَزَادَ: «وَإِذَا زَوَّجَ أَحَدُكُمْ خَادِمَهُ عَبْدَهُ أَوْ أَجِيرَهُ، فَلَا يَنْظُرْ إِلَى مَا دُونَ السُّرَّةِ وَفَوْقَ الرَّكْبَةِ » » (سنن ابوداود بَابُ مَتَى يُؤْمَرُ الْغُلَامُ بِالصَّلَاةِ، غمبر 496)
And a man looks at all of a man's body except what is between his navel and his knee. Evidence: (1) The hadith for establishing: from Amr ibn Shu'ayb, from his father, from his grandfather, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old, and strike them for it when they are ten, and separate them in their beds.' (Sunan Abi Dawud, Chapter on When a Boy is Ordered to Pray, no. 495). Evidence: (2) The hadith for establishing: Dawud ibn Sawwar al-Muzani narrated with his chain and its meaning, and added: 'And when one of you marries his slave-girl to his slave or his hired servant, let him not look at what is below the navel and above the knee.' (Sunan Abi Dawud, Chapter on When a Boy is Ordered to Pray, no. 496).
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage states the ruling regarding a man looking at another man: it is permissible to look at the entire body except the area between the navel and knee, which is the awrah of a man. Two hadith proofs are provided: the first commands separation of children in beds, implying that looking at awrah is prohibited. The second explicitly prohibits looking at what is below the navel and above the knee. Thus, the rest of the body is permissible to look at.

Key Terms
سرة (surrah): Navel; the upper limit of a man's awrah.
ركبة (rukbah): Knee; the lower limit of a man's awrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What part of a man's body is it not permissible for another man to look at?
A) The entire body
B) The area between the navel and knee
C) Only the private parts
D) The chest and thighs

Key Takeaway
A man's awrah towards another man is the area from navel to knee; looking at it is haram, all else is permissible.

Rulings on Looking and Awrah in the Hanafi School
Compilation of rulings on permissible looking at others' bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on permissible looking at others for each category (judge/witness/doctor, man to man, woman to man/woman, mahrams, spouses, slave-women) and identify the supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Looking at awrah is haram, but necessity and relationship create precise exceptions—know the limits for every scenario.

This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar compiles the Hanafi rules on looking (nazar) and the definition of awrah. It begins with exceptions for legal and medical necessity, then outlines general rules for men, women, mahrams, and spouses, each supported by hadith. Understanding these gradations is essential for daily life, from legal testimony to family interactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the persons involved: judge, witness, doctor, men looking at men, women looking at men, women looking at women, man looking at mahrams, man looking at wife/slave-woman.
Each category has a different ruling on what parts may be looked at.
2
State the permissible area for each
For each category, state the exact body parts that can be seen, using the text's explicit limits (e.g., navel to knee for men, face/hands for mahrams).
Pay attention to exceptions: judge/witness can look at face even if desire feared; doctor can look at site of illness.
3
Identify the exceptions for necessity
Note the three necessity exceptions: judge/witness (legal), doctor (medical), and looking at a prospective bride (marriage intent from hadith).
These override the general prohibition even if desire is present.
4
Locate the hadith proofs
For each major ruling, find the corresponding hadith cited in the text. For example, the hadith of Asma for women's awrah, the hadith of Abu Huraira for looking at a bride, etc.
Distinguish between hadith as direct proof and as contextual evidence.
5
Summarize the general principle
From the Urdu principles at the end: covering the body is required, looking at awrah is haram without excuse, looking at non-awrah is permissible when safe from temptation.
This encapsulates the entire section.

Source Text
