كِتَابُ الْحَظُر وَالْإِبَاحَةِ
وَيَجُوزُ لِلْقَاضِي إِذَا أَرَادَ أَنْ يَحْكُمَ عَلَيْهَا وَلِلشَّاهِدِ إِذَا أَرَادَ أَنْ يَشْهَدَ عَلَيْهَا أَنْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَى وَجْهِهَا وَإِنْ خَافَ أَنْ يَشْتَهِيَ
وَيَجُوزُ لِلطَّبِيبِ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَى مَوْضِعِ الْمَرَضِ مِنْهَا
وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ الرَّجُلِ جَمِيعَ بَدَنِهِ إِلَّا مَا بَيْنَ سُرَّتِهِ وَرَكْبَتِهِ
وَيَجُوزُ لِلْمَرْأَةِ أَنْ تَنْظُرَ مِنْ الرَّجُل إِلَى مَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنَ الرَّجُل إِذَا أَمِنَتْ الشَّهْوَةَ
وَتَنْظُرُ الْمَرْأَةُ مِنْ الْمَرْأَةِ إِلَى مَا يَجُوزُ لِلرَّجُل أَنْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ الرَّجُل
وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ أَمَتِهِ الَّتِي تَحِلُّ لَهُ وَمِنْ زَوْجَتِهِ إِلَى فَرْجِهَا
وَيَنْظُرُ الرَّجُلُ مِنْ ذَوَاتِ مَحَارِمِهِ إِلَى الْوَجْهِ وَالرَّأْسِ وَالصَّدْرِ وَالسَّاقَيْنِ وَالْعَضْدَيْنِ
وَعَنْ عَائِشَةَ، أَنَّ أَسْمَاءَ ابْنَةَ أَبِي بَكْرٍ، دَخَلَتْ عَلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَعَلَيْهَا ثِيَابٌ رِقَاقٌ، فَأَعْرَضَ عَنْهَا رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَقَالَ: «يَا أَسْمَاءُ، إِنَّ الْمَرْأَةَ إِذَا بَلَغَتِ الْمَحِيضَ لَمْ تَصْلُحْ أَنْ يُرَى مِنْهَا إِلَّا هَذَا وَهَذَا» وَأَشَارَ إِلَى وَجْهِهِ وَكَفَّيْهِ
وَعَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: كُنْتُ عِنْدَ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَأَتَاهُ رَجُلٌ فَأَخْبَرَهُ أَنَّهُ تَزَوَّجَ امْرَأَةً مِنَ الأَنْصَارِ. فَقَالَ لَهُ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أَنَظَرْتَ إِلَيْهَا؟» قَالَ: لَا. قَالَ: «فَاذْهَبْ فَانْظُرْ إِلَيْهَا، فَإِنَّ فِي أَعْيُنِ الْأَنْصَارِ شَيْئًا»
قُل لَّا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَىَّ مُحَرَّمًا عَلَىٰ طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ إِلَّا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا أَوْ لَحْمَ خِنزِيرٍ فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ أَوْ فِسْقًا أُهِلَّ لِغَيْرِ اللَّهِ بِهِ، فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ غَيْرَ بَاغٍ وَلَا عَادٍ (الأنعام 145)
وَعَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ، عَنْ جَدِّهِ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مُرُوا أَوْلَادَكُمْ بِالصَّلَاةِ وَهُمْ أَبْنَاءُ سَبْعِ سِنِينَ، وَاضْرِبُوهُمْ عَلَيْهَا وَهُمْ أَبْنَاءُ عَشْرٍ، وَفَرِّقُوا بَيْنَهُمْ فِي الْمَضَاجِعِ»
وَحَدَّثَنِي دَاوُدُ بْنُ سَوَّارٍ الْمُزَنِيُّ، بِإِسْنَادِهِ وَمَعْنَاهُ وَزَادَ: «وَإِذَا زَوَّجَ أَحَدُكُمْ خَادِمَهُ عَبْدَهُ أَوْ أَجِيرَهُ، فَلَا يَنْظُرْ إِلَى مَا دُونَ السُّرَّةِ وَفَوْقَ الرَّكْبَةِ»
وَعَنْ عَلِيٍّ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «الرَّكْبَةُ مِنَ الْعَوْرَةِ»
وَعَنْ بَهْزِ بْنِ حَكِيمٍ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ، عَنْ جَدِّهِ، قَالَ: قُلْتُ: يَا نَبِيَّ اللَّهِ عَوْرَاتُنَا مَا نَأْتِي مِنْهَا وَمَا نَذَرُ؟ قَالَ: «احْفَظْ عَوْرَتَكَ إِلَّا مِنْ زَوْجَتِكَ أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ يَمِينُكَ»
وَعَنْ سَعْدِ بْنِ مَسْعُودٍ الْكِنْدِيِّ قَالَ: أَتَى عُثْمَانُ بْنُ مَظْعُونٍ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ إِنِّي لَأَسْتَحْيِي أَنْ تَرَى أَهْلِي عَوْرَتِي قَالَ: «وَقَدْ جَعَلَكَ اللَّهُ لَهُمْ لِبَاسًا، وَجَعَلَهُمْ لَكَ لِبَاسًا» قَالَ: أَكْرَهُ ذَلِكَ قَالَ: «فَإِنَّهُمْ يَرَوْنَهُ مِنِّي، وَأَرَاهُ مِنْهُمْ» قَالَ: أَنْتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ؟ قَالَ: «أَنَا» قَالَ: أَنْتَ، فَمَنْ بَعْدَكَ إِذًا؟ قَالَ: فَلَمَّا أَدْبَرَ عُثْمَانُ قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إِنَّ ابْنَ مَظْعُونٍ لَخَيِّرٌ سَدِيدٌ»
وَعَنْ عُتْبَةَ بْنِ عَبْدٍ السُّلَمِيِّ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إِذَا أَتَى أَحَدُكُمْ أَهْلَهُ فَلْيَسْتَتِرْ، وَلَا يَتَجَرَّدْ تَجَرُّدَ الْعَيْرَيْنِ»
وَقَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَىٰ: ﴿وَلَا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِلَّا مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا﴾
اللَّهُمَّ إِنَّا نَسْأَلُكَ الْعَفْوَ وَالْعَافِيَةَ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَالْآخِرَةِ
أَصُولٌ: عَوْرَةٌ عَامَّةٌ: تَغْطِيَةُ الْجَسَدِ مَطْلُوبَةٌ، وَالنَّظَرُ إِلَى الْعَوْرَةِ حَرَامٌ بِغَيْرِ عُذْرٍ. وَالنَّظَرُ إِلَى مَا عَدَا الْعَوْرَةِ جَائِزٌ عِنْدَ أَمْنِ الْفِتْنَةِ.
Book of Prohibition and Permission. It is permissible for a judge, when he wants to give a ruling against her, and a witness, when he wants to testify against her, to look at her face even if he fears desire. And it is permissible for a doctor to look at the site of illness from her. And a man may look at a man's entire body except what is between his navel and his knee. And it is permissible for a woman to look at a man to the extent that a man may look at a man, if she is safe from desire. And a woman looks at a woman to the extent that a man may look at a man. And a man looks at his slave-woman who is lawful for him and at his wife up to her private parts. And a man looks at his female mahrams at the face, head, chest, shins, and upper arms. And from Aisha that Asma bint Abi Bakr entered upon the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wearing thin clothes, he turned away and said: 'O Asma, when a woman reaches menstruation, it is not proper that anything be seen except this and this,' pointing to his face and hands. And from Abu Huraira that he was with the Prophet and a man came and informed him that he had married a woman from the Ansar; he said 'Did you look at her?' He said no; he said 'Go and look at her, for in the eyes of the Ansar there is something.' Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden for an eater to eat except it be carrion, shed blood, or flesh of swine (al-An'am 145). And from Amr ibn Shuayb from his father from his grandfather: 'Command your children to pray at seven, beat them for it at ten, and separate their beds.' And Dawud ibn Suwar al-Muzani narrated similarly adding: 'And when one of you marries his servant to his slave or hireling, let him not look at what is below the navel and above the knee.' And from Ali: 'The knee is part of the awrah.' And from Bahz ibn Hakim from his father from his grandfather: I said O Prophet of Allah, what of our awrah may we expose and what should we cover? He said: 'Guard your awrah except from your wife or what your right hand possesses.' And from Sa'd ibn Mas'ud al-Kindi that Uthman ibn Maz'un said: O Messenger of Allah, I am shy that my family see my awrah. He said: 'Allah has made you a garment for them and them a garment for you.' He said: I dislike that. He said: 'They see it from me and I see it from them.' He asked: And you, O Messenger of Allah? He said: 'Me.' Then Uthman turned away and the Prophet said: 'Ibn Maz'un is good and upright.' And from Utbah ibn Abd al-Sulami: 'When one of you comes to his wife, let him cover and not disrobe stark naked.' And Allah says: 'And not expose their adornment except what appears thereof.' O Allah, we ask You for forgiveness and well-being in this world and the hereafter. Principles: Covering the body is required; looking at the awrah is forbidden without excuse; looking at other than awrah is permissible when safe from temptation.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings (ahkam) from the Hanafi school on the rules of looking (nazar) and the definition of awrah (parts of the body that must be concealed). The text begins with exceptions: a judge or witness may look at a woman's face for legal purposes, even if he fears desire, because necessity allows it. Similarly, a doctor may look at the site of illness. Then the general rule for men looking at men: everything is permissible except the area from navel to knee, which is awrah. Women may look at men to the same extent as men look at men, but only if safe from desire; this is because the woman's awrah is larger relative to men. For women looking at women, the same limit applies. A man may look at his wife or his slave-woman (with whom relations are lawful) even at the private parts, as there is no restriction between spouses. For mahram women (like mother, sister, etc.), a man may look at the face, head, chest, shins, and upper arms (not the stomach, back, or thighs). The proofs include hadith about Asma showing that a woman must cover except face and hands, a hadith about looking at a prospective bride, Quran verse about necessity overriding prohibition, hadith about separating children's beds and not looking below navel/above knee, hadith that the knee is awrah, hadith about guarding awrah except from spouse, hadith about Uthman ibn Maz'un indicating that spouses can see each other's awrah, and hadith about covering during intercourse. The Urdu principles summarize: covering the body is required, looking at awrah is haram without excuse, and looking at other parts is permissible when safe from temptation. All these rulings are based on the relied-upon positions of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
عورة (awrah): Parts of the body that must be concealed from others; for men: navel to knee; for women: entire body except face and hands (with different rules for mahrams and spouses).
محارم (mahārim): Relatives whom one may never marry, such as mother, sister, daughter, aunt, etc.; looking at their usual areas of body (head, face, etc.) is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how much of a woman's body is considered awrah before non-mahram?
A) Entire body except face and hands
B) From navel to knee only
C) Entire body including face and hands
D) Only private parts

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines awrah relative to the viewer and establishes precise exceptions for necessity, making the rulings on looking a balance between modesty and practical needs.

Women Looking at Men and Women – Hanafi Ruling
Ruling with proof on women's gaze.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman looking at a man or a woman, identify the condition of security from desire, and cite the hadith proof for the limits of male awrah.

Core Idea
A woman may look at a man's body except his awrah (navel to knee) if she is safe from desire; the same applies for woman to woman.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar addresses a frequently asked question: what parts of a man's body may a woman lawfully look at? The ruling extends to a woman looking at another woman. The key condition is security from desire (aman al-shahwah). The Hanafi position is permissive compared to some other schools, and it is grounded in a hadith that defines the awrah boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the matn: it states two rulings – woman looking at man, and woman looking at woman. Both are permissible (mubah) with a condition.
The condition is explicitly 'when she is secure from desire' (إِذَا أَمِنَتِ الشَّهْوَةَ).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'The knee is from the awrah.' This is the proof for the boundary of male awrah.
The hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni; it is a single transmission (ahad) but accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly states that the knee is part of the awrah. Logically, that means the awrah of a man includes only what is between the navel and knee (since the knee is the lower extremity). Everything else – head, arms, legs above knee – is not awrah and therefore permissible to see, provided the condition of no desire is met.
The condition itself (aman al-shahwah) is not from this hadith but from the general principle that looking with desire is forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجُوزُ لِلْمَرْأَةِ أَنْ تَنْظُرَ مِنَ الرَّجُلِ إِلَى مَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ الرَّجُلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنَ الرَّجُلِ إِذَا أَمِنَتِ الشَّهْوَةَ وَتَنْظُرُ الْمَرْأَةُ مِنَ الْمَرْأَةِ إِلَى مَا يَجُوزُ لِلرَّجُلِ أَنْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَيْهِ مِنَ الرَّجُلِ. قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «الرَّكْبَةُ مِنَ الْعَوْرَةِ».
It is permissible for a woman to look from a man at what it is permissible for a man to look at from a man, when she is secure from desire. And a woman looks from a woman at what it is permissible for a man to look at from a man. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The knee is from the private parts.'
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Quduri's matn on permissible looking. It first states that a woman may look at a man's body except the awrah (navel to knee) if she is confident of no desire. The same applies for a woman looking at another woman: permissible to see the same areas a man may see from another man. The hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni establishes that the knee is part of the awrah, thus the awrah of a man is from navel to knee. The Hanafi position is that seeing beyond that is permissible when safe from desire. The usul note in Urdu adds: 'Looking at the face with desire is the zina of the eye, so if there is fear of desire, one should lower the gaze.'

Key Terms
عورة (awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered; for men, from navel to knee.
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; the condition that if present makes looking haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this passage, which body part is explicitly stated to be part of the awrah?
A) The knee
B) The navel
C) The face
D) The hands

Key Takeaway
A woman may look at the non-awrah parts of a man (or another woman) if she is confident of no desire; the awrah limit for men is from navel to knee, proven by the hadith.

Acceptance and Rejection of Executorship and Exception: Ownership by Acceptance
Ruling on valid rejection of executorship and exception to ownership by acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of rejection of executorship and explain the principle that a bequest is owned by acceptance, including the exception where the legatee dies before acceptance.

Core Idea
A bequest is owned only by acceptance, except if the legatee dies before accepting—then it goes to his heirs.

This lesson from Al-Quduri addresses two related issues: the conditions for a valid rejection of an executorship, and the general rule that ownership of a bequest requires the legatee's acceptance. It presents one exception to this rule and supports the rulings with narrations from the Successors and a Companion. Understanding this will clarify when a bequest is considered owned and how the Hanafi school differs from others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage first rules on rejection of executorship (valid only in testator's presence) and then on ownership of bequest (by acceptance, with one exception).
Both rulings are supported by evidence.
2
Locate the primary proof for the ownership rule
The general rule is supported by the practice of the Successors: Ibn 'Awn's consultation with 'Amr and Ibn Sirin's acceptance of executorship.
This indicates acceptance was considered necessary.
3
Identify the exception and its proof
The exception (legatee dies after testator but before acceptance) is proven by the saying of 'Ali that the bequest goes to the legatee's heirs.
This is a Qawl Sahabi.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah of 'Ali's statement
'Ali explicitly states the bequest is for the heirs of the legatee, implying the legatee had a right that passes to his heirs, thus establishing the exception.
The exception deviates from the general rule.
5
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that the legatee owns the bequest immediately upon testator's death without acceptance; the Hanafi school requires acceptance except in this one case.
The difference stems from different usul regarding the nature of wasiyyah.
6
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position as stated in Al-Quduri is the relied-upon view; no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists here.
The zahir al-riwayah is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَوْصَى إِلَى رَجُلٍ فَقَبِلَ الْوَصِيَّةَ فِي وَجْهِ الْمُوصِي وَرَدَّهَا فِي غَيْرِ وَجْهِهِ فَلَيْسَ بِرَدٍّ وَإِنْ رَدَّهَا فِي وَجْهِهِ فَهُوَ رَدٌّ وَتَبْطُلُ الْوَصِيَّةُ. وَالْمُوصَى بِهِ يُمْلَكُ بِالْقَبُولِ إِلَّا فِي مَسْأَلَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهِيَ أَنْ يَمُوتَ الْمُوصِي ثُمَّ يَمُوتُ الْمُوصَى لَهُ قَبْلَ الْقَبُولِ فَيَدْخُلُ الْمُوصَى بِهِ فِي مِلْكِ وَرَثَةِ الْمُوصَى لَهُ. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْمُوصَى بِهِ يُمْلَكُ بِالْقَبُولِ عَنِ ابْنِ عَوْنٍ قَالَ: أَوْصَى إِلَيَّ ابْنُ عَمٍّ لِي فَكَرِهْتُ ذَلِكَ، فَسَأَلْتُ عَمْرًا فَأَمَرَنِي أَنْ أَقْبَلَهَا. قَالَ: وَكَانَ ابْنُ سِيرِينَ يَقْبَلُ الْوَصِيَّةَ. (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ فِي قَبُولِ الْوَصِيَّةِ مَنْ كَانَ يُوصِي إِلَى الرَّجُلِ فَيَقْبَلُ ذَلِكَ، ٣٠٩١٠) وَجْهٌ (٢): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْمُوصَى بِهِ يُمْلَكُ بِالْقَبُولِ عَنْ أَبِي قِلَابَةَ قَالَ: «لَا وَصِيَّةَ لِمَيِّتٍ». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، فِي الرَّجُلِ يُوصِي لِرَجُلٍ بِوَصِيَّةٍ فَيَمُوتُ الْمُوصَى لَهُ قَبْلَ الْمُوصِي، نَمْبَر ٣٠٧٤١) وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ إِلَّا فِي مَسْأَلَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ عَنْ عَلِيٍّ فِي رَجُلٍ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ فَمَاتَ الَّذِي أَوْصَى لَهُ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَأْتِيَهُ، قَالَ: «هِيَ لِوَرَثَةِ الْمُوصَى لَهُ». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ فِي الرَّجُلِ يُوصِي لِرَجُلٍ بِوَصِيَّةٍ فَيَمُوتُ الْمُوصَى لَهُ قَبْلَ الْمُوصِي، نَمْبَر ٣٠٧٣٨ / مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ، ١٦٤٤١) أُصُولٌ: دَمِيتَ [؟] كے بعد موصی لہ مرجائے تو وہ وصیت موصی لہ کے ورثاء کی ملکیت ہو جائے گی۔
And if someone appoints a man as executor and he accepts the executorship in the presence of the testator and rejects it in his absence, it is not a rejection; but if he rejects it in his presence, it is a rejection and the executorship becomes void. And the bequest is owned by acceptance except in one case, which is that the testator dies, then the legatee dies before acceptance, so the bequest enters into the ownership of the legatee's heirs. Evidence (1): saying of the successor to establish 'and the bequest is owned by acceptance' – from Ibn 'Awn who said: My cousin appointed me as executor and I disliked that, so I asked 'Amr and he ordered me to accept it. He said: Ibn Sirin used to accept executorship. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on acceptance of executorship, no. 30910) Evidence (2): saying of the successor to establish 'and the bequest is owned by acceptance' – from Abu Qilabah who said: 'There is no will for a dead person.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on a man who makes a will for a man and the legatee dies before the testator, no. 30741) Evidence (1): saying of the Companion to establish 'except in one case' – from 'Ali regarding a man who made a will for another man and the one for whom he made the will died before it reached him; he said: 'It is for the heirs of the legatee.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 30738 / Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, 16441) Principle: If after the death (of the testator) the legatee dies, then that will becomes the property of the legatee's heirs.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling on the acceptance and rejection of an executorship (wasiyyah) and the ownership of the bequest (musa bihi). The matn from Al-Quduri clarifies that if a person is appointed as executor and he rejects the appointment in the absence of the testator but later accepts in his presence, the rejection is not effective; only a clear rejection in the testator's presence invalidates the executorship. The central principle is that the bequest becomes the property of the legatee only upon his acceptance (qabul). However, there is one exception: if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before he has accepted, the bequest passes to the legatee's heirs, not back to the testator's estate. The text then provides supporting evidence from early authorities. The first evidence from Ibn 'Awn shows that the successor 'Amr and Ibn Sirin encouraged acceptance of executorship. The second from Abu Qilabah states 'There is no will for a dead person', which underscores that the legatee must be alive to accept. The third evidence from 'Ali directly confirms the exception: if the legatee dies before the testator, the bequest goes to his heirs. This ruling is unique to the Hanafi school; the Shafi'i school, for example, holds that the legatee becomes owner upon the testator's death without need for acceptance. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that acceptance is a condition for ownership (tamlik) and that the will is a contract that requires consent. The Urdu note at the end summarizes the exception.

Key Terms
وَصِيَّة (wasiyyah): A will or testament; also used for executorship.
قَبُول (qabul): Acceptance of the executorship or the bequest.
رَدّ (radd): Rejection of the executorship.
مُوصَى بِه (musa bihi): The property or item bequeathed.
مُوصَى لَه (musa lahu): The legatee (person to whom bequest is made).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which scenario does the bequest become the property of the legatee's heirs without the legatee's acceptance?
A) If the legatee is a minor
B) If the legatee dies before the testator
C) If the legatee dies after the testator but before acceptance
D) If the testator revokes the will

Key Takeaway
A bequest in the Hanafi school requires acceptance for ownership, except when the legatee dies after the testator before acceptance, in which case the bequest passes to the legatee's heirs.

Appointing Slave, Disbeliever, or Sinner as Executor
Ruling that judge removes disqualified executors and appoints others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a slave, disbeliever, or sinner as executor, and explain the evidence for removing such an executor.

Core Idea
A testator's will remains valid, but the judge must replace an unqualified executor to protect heirs.

This lesson from Al-Quduri addresses a key question in Islamic estate law: what happens if a testator names an executor who lacks the necessary qualifications? The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: the will itself is not void, but the judge must remove the disqualified person and appoint a suitable replacement. We will examine the ruling and the three pieces of evidence that support it, ensuring you understand both the rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the core statement: the judge removes the slave, disbeliever, or sinner from executorship and appoints others. Note that the will is not invalidated; only the named executor is replaced.
2
Recognize the Three Disqualified Categories
The text explicitly lists three categories: slave (abd), disbeliever (kafir), and flagrant sinner (fasiq). Understand why each is disqualified: slave lacks legal capacity, disbeliever cannot be a guardian over Muslims, and a fasiq is untrustworthy.
3
Analyze Evidence (1): Saying of the Successor
The first evidence is from the tabi'i 'Amir, who compares the executor to a parent and states that if an executor is suspected, he should be removed or have a co-executor appointed. This establishes the principle of removing untrustworthy executors.
4
Analyze Evidence (2): Saying of the Companion
The second evidence is from Ibn Abbas, who said that a slave cannot make a will. This implies a slave cannot be an executor because he lacks independent authority over property.
5
Analyze Evidence (3): Quranic Prohibition
The third evidence is Quran 4:144, which prohibits taking disbelievers as awliya (guardians/allies). The Hanafi school extends this to executors, as they act as guardians over the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدٍ أَوْ كَافِرٍ أَوْ فَاسِقٍ أَخْرَجَهُمُ الْقَاضِي مِنَ الْوَصِيَّةِ وَنَصَبَ غَيْرَهُمْ. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدٍ أَوْ كَافِرٍ عَنْ عَامِرٍ قَالَ: «الْوَصِيُّ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَالِدِ، وَإِذَا اتُّهِمَ الْوَصِيُّ عُزِلَ أَوْ جُعِلَ مَعَهُ غَيْرُهُ». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، مَنْ قَالَ: وَصِيَّةُ الْعَبْدِ حَيْثُ جَعَلَهَا، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٧٤) وَجْهٌ (٢): قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدٍ أَوْ كَافِرٍ عَنْ جُنْدَبٍ قَالَ: سَأَلَ طَهْمَانُ ابْنَ عَبَّاسٍ: أَيُوصِي الْعَبْدُ؟ قَالَ: «لَا». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٧٢ / مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ، ١٦٤٦٥) وَجْهٌ (٣): الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدٍ أَوْ كَافِرٍ: {يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا الْكَافِرِينَ أَوْلِيَاءَ مِنْ دُونِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ أَتُرِيدُونَ أَنْ تَجْعَلُوا لِلَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ سُلْطَانًا مُبِينًا} [سورة النساء: ١٤٤]
And whoever appoints a slave, a disbeliever, or a sinner as executor, the judge removes them from the executorship and appoints others in their place. Evidence (1): saying of the successor to establish 'and whoever appoints a slave or a disbeliever' – from 'Amir who said: 'The executor is like a parent, and if the executor is suspected, he is removed or someone else is appointed with him.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on the will of a slave, no. 30874) Evidence (2): saying of the Companion to establish 'and whoever appoints a slave or a disbeliever' – from Jundab who said: Tahman asked Ibn 'Abbas: Can a slave make a will? He said: 'No.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 30872 / Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, 16465) Evidence (3): the verse to establish 'and whoever appoints a slave or a disbeliever': {O you who believe! Do not take the disbelievers as allies instead of the believers. Do you wish to give Allah a clear proof against yourselves?} [Surah an-Nisa: 144]
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This lesson addresses the qualifications of an executor (wasi). According to Hanafi law, if a testator appoints a slave, a disbeliever (kafir), or a flagrant sinner (fasiq) as executor, such appointment is not valid; the judge (qadi) must remove that person and appoint a suitable executor in his place. The text provides three pieces of evidence. First, the saying of the successor 'Amir: 'The executor is like a parent' – indicating the high responsibility of an executor, and if he is suspect, he should be removed or have a co-executor appointed. Second, Ibn 'Abbas categorically stated that a slave cannot make a will, meaning he cannot be an executor because a slave lacks full legal capacity over his own affairs. Third, the Quranic verse 4:144 prohibits taking disbelievers as awliya (guardians/allies); since an executor acts as a guardian over the estate and heirs, a disbeliever cannot serve in that role. The Hanafi school thus protects the interests of the heirs by ensuring the executor is trustworthy and capable. Note that the judge does not nullify the will itself; he simply replaces the disqualified executor.

Key Terms
عَبْد (abd): A slave; someone who lacks full legal capacity to act as executor.
كَافِر (kafir): A disbeliever; one who is not a Muslim. Cannot be executor over Muslim heirs.
فَاسِق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins openly; one whose trustworthiness is compromised.
قَاضِي (qadi): The judge who removes the disqualified executor and appoints a suitable replacement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a testator appoints a disbeliever as executor, what action does the judge take?
A) The will becomes void
B) The disbeliever remains executor but with supervision
C) The judge removes the disbeliever and appoints another executor
D) The judge cancels the entire will

Key Takeaway
A will is not invalidated by naming an unqualified executor; the judge removes the disqualified person and appoints a proper replacement.

Invalidity of Appointing Own Slave as Executor with Adult Heirs
Ruling that own-slave appointment invalid with adult heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing one's own slave as executor when adult heirs exist, and explain the evidence from the Companion Ibn 'Abbas.

Core Idea
Appointing your own slave as executor is invalid when adult heirs are present because it is harmful.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Islamic inheritance law: a testator appoints his own slave as the executor of his will, while there are adult heirs present. The Hanafi school rules that such an appointment is invalid. The key reason is that causing harm (dirar) in a will is a major sin, and this appointment is considered harmful to the adult heirs because the slave lacks the legal capacity to act independently. The evidence is drawn from the saying of Ibn 'Abbas, who linked the prohibition of harm in wills to the Quranic verse in Surah an-Nisa (4:12). Understanding this ruling helps clarify the conditions for a valid executor appointment in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is appointing one's own slave as executor. The ruling in this passage is that it is invalid (lam tasihh) when adult heirs exist.
The ruling is conditional on the presence of adult heirs.
2
Identify the condition that triggers invalidity
The text specifies 'while among the heirs there are adults' (wa fi al-warathati kibar). The presence of adult heirs is the condition that renders the appointment invalid.
If no adult heirs (e.g., only minors), the ruling might differ, but the text does not address that.
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence is a saying (qawl) of the Companion Ibn 'Abbas, who stated that causing harm (sirar) in a will is among the major sins, and recited Quran 4:12 which commands 'without harming'.
The dalil is from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the evidence proves the ruling)
Ibn 'Abbas's statement establishes that harming heirs through a will is a major sin. Appointing one's own slave as executor when adult heirs exist is deemed harmful because the slave cannot manage the estate independently, potentially causing harm to the heirs.
The Hanafi school uses this to invalidate the appointment as a harmful provision.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
The Hanafi position is that the appointment is invalid. Some other schools may allow it if the slave is competent, but the Hanafi view prioritizes the harm principle.
This ikhtilaf is acknowledged in the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدِ نَفْسِهِ وَفِي الْوَرَثَةِ كِبَارٌ لَمْ تَصِحَّ الْوَصِيَّةُ. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى عَبْدِ نَفْسِهِ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: «الصِّرَارُ فِي الْوَصِيَّةِ مِنَ الْكَبَائِرِ»، ثُمَّ تَلَا: {غَيْرَ مُضَارٍّ وَصِيَّةً مِنَ اللَّهِ} [النساء: ١٢] (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، مَنْ كَانَ يُوصِي وَيَسْتَحِبُّهَا، نَمْبَر ٣٠٩٣٣)
And whoever appoints his own slave as executor while among the heirs there are adults, the executorship is not valid. Evidence (1): saying of the Companion to establish 'and whoever appoints his own slave' – from Ibn 'Abbas who said: 'Harm (dirar) in a will is among the major sins.' Then he recited: {without harming, a will from Allah} [an-Nisa: 12]. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on who used to make a will and considered it commendable, no. 30933)
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This short lesson deals with a specific scenario: a testator appoints his own slave as executor, and there are adult heirs. According to the Hanafi ruling, such an appointment is invalid (lam tasihh al-wasiyyah). The reason is that the slave is owned by the testator and lacks independent legal capacity; moreover, the presence of adult heirs requires a capable executor who is not a mere extension of the testator's property. The evidence is from Ibn 'Abbas, who stated that causing harm (dirar) in a will is a major sin, and then recited the Quranic verse from Surah an-Nisa (4:12) which instructs that a will be made 'without harm.' Appointing a slave as executor when adult heirs exist is considered harmful to the heirs because the slave may not be able to act in their best interest. The implication is that if there were no adult heirs (e.g., only minors), the appointment might be allowed? The text is silent, but the condition here explicitly mentions adult heirs, so the ruling is conditional.

Key Terms
عَبْد نَفْسِه (abd nafsihi): One's own slave (the slave of the testator).
كِبَار (kibar): Adult heirs (as opposed to minors).
صِرَار (sirar): Harm or injury (in the context of wills).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is the appointment of one's own slave as executor invalid?
A) Always, regardless of heirs
B) Only if the slave is a disbeliever
C) When there are adult heirs among the heirs
D) When the testator has no other suitable executor

Key Takeaway
The appointment of one's own slave as executor is invalid when adult heirs exist because it constitutes harmful executorship, which is prohibited by the Quran and the saying of Ibn 'Abbas.

Judge Appointing Co-Executor When Executor Is Incapable
Ruling that judge appoints another alongside an incapable executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an executor is incapable and explain the judge's action and its evidence.

Core Idea
An incapable executor is supplemented, not removed, preserving the testator's choice.

What happens when a testator appoints an executor who later becomes incapable of managing the estate? The Hanafi school, as recorded by Al-Quduri, provides a nuanced solution: the judge appoints a co-executor alongside the incapable one, rather than removing him entirely. This lesson examines the ruling, its evidentiary basis from the statement of the successor 'Amir, and the school's preference for this less intrusive measure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
Read the matn: 'And whoever appoints someone who is incapable of carrying out the executorship, the judge appoints another person with him.' The ruling applies when the executor is physically or mentally incapable. The judge's action is to add a co-executor, not to remove the original.
Incapacity includes illness, old age, or incompetence.
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The author cites the statement of 'Amir al-Sha'bi: 'The executor is like a parent, and if the executor is suspected, he is removed or someone else is appointed with him.' This is a qawl tabi'i (saying of a successor) recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq.
The term 'suspected' (ittuhima) here is interpreted by Hanafis to include incapacity, not just dishonesty.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the evidence proves the ruling)
The statement 'he is removed or someone else is appointed with him' gives the judge a choice. The Hanafi school selects the option of appointment (damma) because it preserves the testator's choice to some extent and is less drastic than removal. This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference).
Other schools may prefer removal; the Hanafi approach is a middle path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى مَنْ يَعْجِزُ عَنِ الْقِيَامِ بِالْوَصِيَّةِ ضَمَّ إِلَيْهِ الْقَاضِي غَيْرَهُ. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمِنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى مَنْ يَعْجِزُ عَنْ الْقِيَامِ بِالْوَصِيَّةِ عَنْ عَامِرٍ قَالَ: الْوَصِيُّ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْوَالِدِ، وَإِذَا اتُّهِمَ الْوَصِيُّ عُزِلَ أَوْ جُعِلَ مَعَهُ غَيْرُهُ. (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، مَنْ قَالَ: وَصِيَّةُ الْعَبْدِ حَيْثُ جَعَلَهَا، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٧٤ / مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ، ١٦٤٨٦)
And whoever appoints someone who is incapable of carrying out the executorship, the judge appoints another person with him. Evidence (1): saying of the successor to establish 'and whoever appoints someone who is incapable' – from 'Amir who said: 'The executor is like a parent, and if the executor is suspected, he is removed or someone else is appointed with him.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on the will of a slave, no. 30874 / Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, 16486)
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This lesson deals with an executor who is physically or mentally incapable of fulfilling his duties (e.g., due to illness, old age, or incompetence). The Hanafi ruling is that the judge (qadi) does not remove such an executor entirely; rather, he appoints a co-executor to work alongside him (damma ilayhi ghayrahu). This preserves the testator's choice to some extent while ensuring the estate is properly managed. The evidence cited is the same saying of the successor 'Amir that appeared in lesson 2: the executor is like a parent, and if he is suspect (including being incapable), he may be removed or a co-executor appointed. The judge's role is to rectify the situation with minimal interference. This reflects the Hanafi principle of istihsan, preferring a solution that balances the testator's intention with the welfare of the heirs.

Key Terms
يَعْجِز (ya'jiz): To be incapable; unable to perform the duties of executorship.
ضَمَّ (damma): To join or appoint alongside; the judge adds a co-executor.
قَاضِي (qadi): The judge who has authority to appoint a co-executor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a testator appoints an incapable executor, what action does the judge take according to the Hanafi school?
A) The judge removes the executor and appoints a new one
B) The judge leaves the executor alone despite the incapacity
C) The judge appoints another person to assist the executor
D) The judge nullifies the entire will

Key Takeaway
When an executor is incapable, the Hanafi school instructs the judge to appoint a co-executor, not to remove the original, based on the flexible option given in the saying of 'Amir.

Joint Executors: When May One Act Alone?
Ruling on joint executors with list of exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint executors and identify the eight exceptions where one executor may act alone without the other.

Core Idea
Two executors must act jointly; only eight urgent matters allow one to act alone without the other.

When a testator appoints two executors, they are co‑trustees of the estate. Their authority is shared, and neither may act independently unless the law grants an exception. This lesson presents the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the limited list of actions that one executor may perform alone. These exceptions cover urgent necessities that cannot wait for joint consultation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text states that it is not permissible for one of two appointed executors to act without the other. This is the default position – joint authority is required.
The ruling applies to both living and deceased testators.
2
List the eight exceptions
The text enumerates eight matters where an individual executor may act alone: buying a shroud and preparing the deceased, providing food and clothing for minors, returning a specific deposit, paying a debt due to the deceased, executing a specific will, freeing a specific slave, and litigating on behalf of the deceased.
These are considered urgent or routine and require immediate attention.
3
Locate the dalil
The evidence cited is a saying of Umar: 'When there is a will and manumission, they take proportionate shares.' This is from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah. It supports the idea that multiple claims must be dealt with jointly or proportionally.
The dalil is zanni in both transmission and meaning, but it lends general support.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text specifies 'according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.' This implies Abu Yusuf may have a broader view allowing more individual actions. The mufta bihi is the stricter view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The difference affects the scope of authority in practice.
5
Evaluate the practical implication
Understanding these exceptions prevents invalid transactions. If an executor acts alone outside the list, the act is not permissible and likely void (batil).
Always check if the action falls under the eight exceptions before acting singly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى اثْنَيْنِ ثُمَّ لَا يَجُوزُ لِأَحَدِهِمَا أَنْ يَتَصَرَّفَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ دُونَ صَاحِبِهِ إِلَّا فِي شِرَاءِ الْكَفَنِ لِلْمَيِّتِ وَتَجْهِيزِهِ وَطَعَامِ الصِّغَارِ وَكِسْوَتِهِمْ وَرَدِّ وَدِيعَةٍ بِعَيْنِهَا وَقَضَاءِ دَيْنٍ عَلَيْهِ وَتَنْفِيدِ وَصِيَّةٍ بِعَيْنِهَا أَوْ عِتْقِ عَبْدٍ بِعَيْنِهِ وَالْخُصُومَةِ فِي حَقِّ الْمَيِّتِ. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَى اثْنَيْنِ عَنْ عُمَرَ قَالَ: «إِذَا كَانَتْ وَصِيَّةٌ وَعَتَاقَةٌ تَحَاصُّوا». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، مَنْ كَانَ يُوصِي وَيَسْتَحِبُّهَا، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٧٥)
And whoever appoints two executors, then it is not permissible for one of them to act without his partner according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, except in buying the shroud for the deceased and preparing him, and food for the minors and their clothing, and returning a specific deposit, and paying a debt due to him, and executing a specific will, or freeing a specific slave, and litigation in the right of the deceased. Evidence (1): saying of the Companion to establish 'and whoever appoints two executors' – from 'Umar who said: 'When there is a will and manumission, they take proportionate shares.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on who used to make a will and considered it commendable, no. 30875)
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This lesson addresses the situation where a testator appoints two joint executors (wasayyan). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), neither executor may act alone without the consent of the other, except in eight specified matters that are deemed urgent or routine: (1) buying a shroud and preparing the deceased for burial; (2) providing food and clothing for minor heirs; (3) returning a specific deposit to its owner; (4) paying a debt owed by the deceased; (5) executing a specific bequest (wasiyyah); (6) freeing a specific slave (who may have been promised freedom); and (7) engaging in litigation on behalf of the deceased's rights (e.g., claiming debts). The evidence from 'Umar is about proportionate shares when there are competing claims (wills and manumissions), which is a different context but is cited as a general support for the principle of dividing rights. Note that Abu Yusuf may have a different view (implied by 'according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad'), possibly allowing one executor to act alone more freely. The rulings aims to prevent abuse of authority while allowing necessary actions that cannot be delayed. The listed exceptions are actions that are clearly beneficial and often time-sensitive.

Key Terms
اثْنَيْن (ithnayn): Two executors appointed jointly.
يَتَصَرَّف (yatasarrif): To act or administer the estate.
كَفَن (kafan): Shroud for the deceased.
وَدِيعَة (wadi'ah): A deposit; returning a specific deposit is allowed without co-executor's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, in which of the following may one of two joint executors act alone?
A) Selling a house from the estate
B) Buying a shroud for the deceased
C) Investing estate funds
D) Transferring property to an heir

Key Takeaway
Two joint executors must act together; only eight urgent matters—shroud, food/clothing for minors, return deposit, pay debt, execute will, free slave, litigation—allow one to act alone.

Proportional Division of Bequest Among Multiple Legatees
Ruling on dividing one-third bequest among multiple legatees when heirs do not approve excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the proportional distribution of a one-third bequest among multiple legatees when heirs do not approve, using the ratio of their prescribed shares.

Core Idea
When heirs do not approve excess, the one-third is divided proportionally among legatees, not equally unless shares are equal.

A testator may name multiple beneficiaries with shares that total more than one-third. Without the heirs' approval, only one-third of the estate can be given. This lesson explains how that one-third is split among the legatees according to the ratio of their prescribed shares, ensuring justice to all parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify total bequest and the one-third limitation
Recognize that the testator's wishes add up to more than one-third, but without heir approval, only one-third is available for distribution.
2
Determine the prescribed shares of each legatee
Identify the fraction of the estate the testator intended for each legatee (e.g., 1/3 to each, or 1/3 and 1/6). These are the 'prescribed shares' that form the basis of proportionality.
3
Convert prescribed shares to a ratio
Express the prescribed shares as a simple integer ratio. For equal shares (1/3 and 1/3), the ratio is 1:1. For 1/3 and 1/6, the ratio is 2:1 (since 1/3 : 1/6 = 2:1).
4
Divide the one-third according to the ratio
Split the one-third into parts equal to the sum of the ratio numbers. Then allocate that number of parts to each legatee accordingly. For ratio 1:1, half each. For ratio 2:1, two parts to the legatee intended a third, one part to the legatee intended a sixth.
The actual share of the estate each receives = (parts / total ratio parts) × (1/3).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ وَلِآخَرَ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ وَلَمْ تُجِزِ الْوَرَثَةُ فَالثُّلُثُ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَانِ. فَإِنْ أَوْصَى لِأَحَدِهِمَا بِالتُّلْثِ وَلِلْآخَرِ بِالسُّدُسِ وَلَمْ تُجِزِ الْوَرَثَةُ فَالتُّلْثُ بَيْنَهُمَا أَثْلَاثًا. وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ عَنِ الزُّهْرِيِّ، فِي رَجُلٍ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ بِفَرَسٍ وَسَمَّاهُ، وَقَالَ: ثُلُثُ مَالِي لِفُلَانٍ وَفُلَانٍ، وَكَانَ الْفَرَسُ ثُلُثَ مَالِهِ، قَالَ الزُّهْرِيُّ: نَرَى أَنْ يُقْسَمَ ثُلُثَ مَالِهِ عَلَى حِصَصِهِمْ. (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، رَجُلٌ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ بِفَرَسٍ، وَأَوْصَى لِآخَرَ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ، وَكَانَ الْفَرَسُ ثُلُثَ مَالِهِ، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٦٨) وَجْهٌ (١): قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ فَإِنْ أَوْصَى لِأَحَدِهِمَا بِالثُّلُثِ عَنِ الْحَسَنِ، أَنَّهُ قَالَ فِي رَجُلٍ أَوْصَى بِدِرْهَمٍ وَبِالسُّدُسِ وَنَحْوِهِ، قَالَ: «يَتَحَاصُّونَ جَمِيعًا». (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، رَجُلٌ أَوْصَى لِرَجُلٍ بِفَرَسٍ، وَأَوْصَى لِآخَرَ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ، وَكَانَ الْفَرَسُ ثُلُثَ مَالِهِ، نَمْبَر ٣٠٨٦٩) أُصُولٌ: اگر میت کے وارثین اجازت نہ دیں تو سبھی قرض خواہوں کو ایک تہائی میں سے لینا ہو گا۔ أُصُولٌ: اگر میت نے صراحتا تہائی مال سے زیادہ کی وصیت کی تو از خود باطل ہو کر تہائی پر آجائے گی۔
And whoever bequeaths to one man one-third of his wealth and to another one-third of his wealth, and the heirs do not approve, then the third is between them half and half. If he bequeaths to one of them a third and to the other a sixth, and the heirs do not approve, then the third is between them in three parts (two shares to the one intended a third, one share to the one intended a sixth). Evidence (1): saying of the successor to establish 'and whoever bequeaths to a man one-third of his wealth' – from al-Zuhri regarding a man who bequeathed a specific horse and said: one-third of my wealth is for so-and-so and so-and-so, and the horse was one-third of his wealth; al-Zuhri said: I see that his one-third wealth should be divided according to their shares. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on a man who bequeaths a horse and another one-third, no. 30868) Evidence (1): saying of the successor to establish 'if he bequeaths to one of them a third' – from al-Hasan who said regarding a man who bequeathed a dirham and a sixth and the like: 'They all take proportionate shares.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 30869) Principles: If the heirs of the deceased do not give permission, then all the creditors must take from one-third. Principle: If the deceased explicitly bequeaths more than one-third of the wealth, it is automatically void and becomes one-third.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This lesson explains how the one-third bequest is divided when there are multiple legatees and the heirs do not approve the excess (since a bequest cannot exceed one-third without heir consent). The matn gives two examples: (1) If the testator bequeaths one-third to each of two persons, and the heirs do not approve, the one-third is divided equally between them (half each), because their prescribed shares are equal. (2) If he bequeaths one-third to one and one-sixth to another, and the heirs do not approve, the one-third is divided into three parts: the one who was to get one-third receives two parts, and the one who was to get one-sixth receives one part (i.e., proportional to their shares: 1/3 : 1/6 = 2:1). The evidence from al-Zuhri supports the principle of proportional division: the bequest is distributed according to the shares specified. Al-Hasan's statement confirms that multiple legatees take proportionate shares (tahassu). The Urdu principles at the end summarize: (1) When heirs do not approve, all creditors take from the one-third; and (2) If the testator explicitly bequeaths more than one-third, the excess is automatically void and the bequest is limited to one-third. The latter is a standard Hanafi ruling: wills exceeding one-third are not valid without heir consent, but the text here deals with cases where the total bequest is within one-third but divided among multiple legatees.

Key Terms
ثُلُث (thuluth): One-third of the estate, the maximum a testator can bequeath without heir consent.
إِجَازَة (ijazah): Permission/approval of the heirs for the bequest to exceed one-third.
تَحَاصُّوا (tahassu): They take proportionate shares; division is proportional to prescribed amounts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a testator bequeaths one-third to one person and one-sixth to another, and the heirs do not approve, how is the one-third divided?
A) Equally between them
B) The one-third is void and nothing is given
C) Two-thirds of the one-third to the one intended a third and one-third of the one-third to the one intended a sixth
D) The one-third goes entirely to the one intended a third

Key Takeaway
The one-third bequest is divided proportionally among legatees based on their prescribed shares when heirs do not approve excess.

8 min
Distributing a Bequest of All and One-Third
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bequest distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on distributing a one-third bequest between two legatees when heirs reject the excess, and identify the exception to Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
Heirs rejecting bequest beyond one-third: Abu Hanifa splits equally; Sahibayn split 3:1 for the legatee of all.

When a testator bequeaths all his property to one person and one-third to another, heirs often refuse the excess beyond one-third. How should the available one-third be split between the two legatees? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, with the majority position (Sahibayn) prevailing in fatwa. This lesson walks through the scenario, the two rulings, and an exception Abu Hanifa makes for certain transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal limit
Recognize the testator's two bequests: all property to one, one-third to another. Heirs refuse the excess beyond one-third. Therefore only one-third of the estate is available for distribution between the two legatees.
The legal limit is the 'one-third rule' for bequests when heirs do not consent.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa divides the one-third into two equal halves (1/2 each). He treats both legatees equally, ignoring the original amounts.
His reasoning: the excess is invalid, so each legatee's claim reduces to the same share within the permitted one-third.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad divide the one-third into four shares: three for the legatee of all property, one for the legatee of one-third. Ratio 3:1.
They consider the original entitlements: the legatee of all was due the whole, so he gets three parts relative to the other's one part.
4
Note the exception in Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa makes an exception for three types of transactions (muhabah, sa'aya, mursala dirhams). In those, he does give the legatee what exceeds the one-third, i.e., he does not apply the equal split.
This exception is narrow and context-specific; the normal rule remains equal halves for ordinary bequests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ أَوْصَى لِأَحَدِهِمَا بِجَمِيعِ مَالِهِ وَلِلْآخَرِ بِثُلُثِ مَالِهِ فَلَمْ تُجِزُ الْوَرَثَةُ فَالثُّلْثُ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَسْهُم عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ الثُّلُثُ بَيْنَهُمَا نِصْفَانِ وَلَا يَضْرِبُ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ لِلْمُوصَى لَهُ بِمَا زَادَ عَلَى الثَّلُثِ إِلَّا فِي الْمُحَابَاةِ وَالسَّعَايَةِ وَالدَّرَاهِمِ الْمُرْسَلَةِ
If he bequeaths to one of them all his property and to the other one-third of his property, and the heirs do not permit, then the one-third is between them into four shares according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Hanifa said: The one-third is between them halves, and Abu Hanifa does not give the legatee what is over the one-third except in muhabah, sa'aya, and mursala dirhams.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario: a testator leaves all his property to one person and one-third to another, but the heirs do not approve the bequest exceeding one-third. The ruling centers on distributing the one-third between the two legatees. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the one-third is divided into four shares: the legatee of the whole property gets three shares (since he was entitled to all but only receives one-third) and the other gets one share (his original one-third). Abu Hanifa, however, says the one-third is split equally (halves) between them. Additionally, Abu Hanifa makes an exception for certain transactions (muhabah, sa'aya, mursala dirhams) where he does allow including the excess in the calculation. The passage type is a ruling statement with an intra-madhhab difference, typical of Al-Quduri's matn.

Key Terms
مُحَابَاة (muhābah): A sale or exchange where the price is reduced below market value, considered a gift-like transaction in death illness.
سَعَايَة (sa‘āyah): Compensation paid by a partially manumitted slave to earn freedom; in this context, a transaction in death illness.
دَرَاهِم مُرْسَلَة (darāhim mursalah): Dirhams given without specific return, akin to a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, when a person bequeaths all his property to one and one-third to another, and heirs do not permit, how is the one-third divided?
A) Equally between the two legatees
B) Into four shares: three for the legatee of all, one for the other
C) The legatee of all gets nothing
D) The one-third goes entirely to the legatee of one-third

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for splitting a bequest limited to one-third between two legatees is the Sahibayn's 3:1 ratio, not Abu Hanifa's equal split.

Bequest When Debt Encompasses Property
Ruling on bequest when debt covers all

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a bequest when the testator's debt covers all assets, and identify the exception that allows the bequest to take effect.

Core Idea
Debt takes absolute priority over bequests; a bequest is void when debt exhausts the estate, unless creditors forgive.

This short but powerful ruling from the Quduri matn establishes a fundamental priority in Islamic inheritance: debt repayment comes before any bequests. If a person dies with debts that equal or exceed his entire property, his bequests are ineffective because the creditors have the first right to the estate. Only their voluntary forgiveness can unlock the bequest. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on safeguarding creditors' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act under consideration is a bequest (wasiyya) made by a person who has debt that encompasses his entire property – i.e., the debt covers all assets.
The bequest itself is a voluntary charity promised after death.
2
State the default ruling
The default ruling is that such a bequest does not take effect – it is void. The phrase 'lam tajri al-wasiyyah' means it is not executed.
The bequest is not merely suspended; it has no legal effect without the exception.
3
Identify the exception
The exception is when the creditors (ghuramā') release the debtor from the debt – meaning they forgive the debt entirely. Only then does the bequest become valid.
Creditors' release essentially frees up the estate for the bequest.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The principle is that debt repayment (dayn) takes priority over bequests (wasiyya) and inheritance (irth). This is based on Quranic verses (e.g., 4:11-12) and ijma'.
This ruling is a specific application of the general rule that debts must be settled before any distribution to heirs or beneficiaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى وَعَلَيْهِ دَيْنٌ يُحِيطُ بِمَالِهِ لَم تَجْرُ الْوَصِيَّةُ إلَّا أَنْ يُبْرِئَهُ الْغُرَمَاءُ مِنْ الدَّيْنِ
And whoever makes a bequest while he has debt that encompasses his property, the bequest does not take effect unless the creditors release him from the debt.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This is a clear ruling: if a deceased person owes debts equal to or greater than his entire estate, any bequests he made are invalid because creditors' rights take precedence. The bequest only becomes effective if the creditors voluntarily forgive the debt (thus freeing up the estate). This reflects the Hanafi principle that debt repayment precedes bequests and inheritance.

Key Terms
يُحِيطُ بِمَالِهِ (yuḥīṭu bi-mālihī): Debt that completely covers or exceeds the entire property of the deceased.
الْغُرَمَاءُ (al-ghuramā'): The creditors; the people to whom the deceased owes money.
لَم تَجْرُ الْوَصِيَّةُ (lam tajri al-wasiyyah): The bequest does not take effect; it is not executed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a person dies with debt equal to his entire property, what happens to his bequests?
A) They are valid from one-third
B) They are void unless creditors forgive
C) They are valid from the whole estate
D) They are split with heirs

Key Takeaway
A bequest is void when the testator's debt covers all assets, unless all creditors forgive the debt.

Manumission and Muhaba in Death Illness: The Priority Dispute
Rulings on freeing slaves and favored sales during death illness, with intra-Hanafi priority dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for manumission and muhabah in death illness, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over priority between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In death illness, manumission and muhabah compete; Sahibayn prioritize manumission, Abu Hanifa prioritizes the earlier act.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses the legal treatment of manumission, muhabah, and gifts made during a terminal illness (marad al-mawt). While all are valid from the one-third of the estate, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises when these acts compete for limited funds. Understanding this dispute reveals how the school balances temporal sequencing with the religious merit of freeing slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their shared ruling
Recognize that manumission, muhabah, and gifts during death illness are valid but limited to one-third of the estate and compete with other bequests.
2
Recognize the competition scenario
When the one-third is insufficient to honor all such acts, a priority order is needed. The dispute centers on how to allocate.
The passage assumes the one-third is the total allowable bequest.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa gives priority to the earlier act: if muhabah preceded manumission, muhabah is fulfilled first; if manumission preceded, they are treated equally.
Temporal priority is the governing principle, with an exception when manumission is first.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give manumission absolute priority in both sequences.
They view manumission as a act of religious devotion that should be favored regardless of order.
5
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
Later Hanafi practice (mufta bihi) follows the Sahibayn due to the superior religious merit of manumission.
Both positions are from Zahir al-Riwayah, but the Sahibayn's view prevailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَعْتَقَ عَبْدًا فِي مَرَضِهِ أَوْ بَاعَ وَحَابَى أَوْ وَهَبَ فَذَلِكَ كُلُّهُ جَائِزٌ وَهُوَ مُعْتَبَرٌ مِنَ الثُّلُثِ وَيَضْرِبُ بِهِ مَعَ أَصْحَابِ الْوَصَايَا فَإِنْ حَابَى ثُمَّ أَعْتَقَ فَالْمُحَابَاةُ أَوْلَى عِنْدَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ فَإِنْ أَعْتَقَ ثُمَّ حَابَى فَهُمَا سَوَاءٌ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدُ الْعِتْقُ أَوْلَى فِي الْمَسْأَلَتَيْنِ
And whoever frees a slave during his illness, or sells with muhabah, or gifts, all that is permissible and it is reckoned from the one-third, and it competes with other bequests. If he made muhabah then freed, then muhabah has priority according to Abu Hanifa. If he freed then made muhabah, then they are equal. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Manumission has priority in both cases.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage covers actions taken in death illness (marad al-mawt) that are treated as bequests: manumission, muhabah (selling below market price), and gifts. All are valid but subject to the one-third limit. If the estate cannot cover both manumission and muhabah, a priority dispute arises. Abu Hanifa gives priority to the earlier act: if muhabah preceded, it comes first; if manumission preceded, they are treated equally. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad always give manumission priority, reasoning that freeing a slave is an act of religious devotion that should be favored. This shows a clash between the principle of temporal priority and special merit of manumission.

Key Terms
حَابَى (ḥābā): To sell at a price below market value, considered a form of donation in death illness.
مَرَض الْمَوْت (marad al-mawt): Deadly illness where death is probable, subject to special rules for transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if a person in death illness sells an item for less than its value (muhabah) and later frees a slave, which has priority?
A) The muhabah
B) They are equal
C) The manumission
D) Neither, all are void

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon view is that manumission in death illness always takes priority over muhabah and other bequests, regardless of the order.

Bequest of an Indeterminate 'Share' (Sahm)
Ruling on indeterminate bequest of 'a share' of property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a testator bequeaths 'a share' of property and apply the rule of minimum one-sixth.

Core Idea
A bequest of 'a share' means the smallest heir's share, but never less than one-sixth.

The term 'sahm' (share) in a will is ambiguous. How does Hanafi law interpret it? The jurists ruled that it refers to the least share any heir receives, but with a floor of one-sixth to ensure the legatee receives a meaningful benefit. This lesson explains the ruling and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
The testator uses 'a share' (sahm) without specifying a fraction. Recognize that this is an undefined bequest.
2
State the default interpretation
Hanafi law interprets 'share' as the smallest share that any legal heir would receive from the estate.
This is based on the principle of favoring the legatee within the limits of the law.
3
Apply the minimum safeguard
If the smallest heir share is less than one-sixth, the legatee receives one-sixth instead. The legatee never gets less than one-sixth.
One-sixth is the minimum share in many inheritance contexts, ensuring a decent benefit.
4
Work through examples
Apply the rule to different heir compositions. Compute the smallest share, compare to one-sixth, and determine the legatee's share.
Examples: two sons → smallest share 1/2 (≥1/6) → legatee gets 1/2; son and daughter → daughter's 1/3 → legatee gets 1/3; wife and three sons → wife's 1/8 (<1/6) → legatee gets 1/6.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى بِسَهْمٍ مِنْ مَالِهِ فَلَهُ أَخَسُّ سِهَامِ الْوَرَثَةِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَنْقُصَ عَنِ السُّدُسِ فَيُتِمُّ لَهُ السُّدُسَ
And whoever bequeaths a share (sahm) of his property, then he receives the lowest share of the heirs, unless it falls below one-sixth, then it is completed to one-sixth for him.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
When a testator says 'I give a share of my property,' the term 'share' is ambiguous. Hanafi law interprets it as the smallest share any heir receives, but with a minimum of one-sixth. For example, if heirs are two sons, the smallest share is half (but half is above one-sixth, so he gets half). If the smallest share were less than one-sixth (like one-eighth), the legatee gets one-sixth. This ensures a minimum benefit. The ruling reflects careful interpretation of ambiguous expressions in wills.

Key Terms
سَهْم (sahm): An undefined portion or share of property in a will.
أَخَسُّ سِهَامِ الْوَرَثَةِ (akhassu sihaam al-warathah): The lowest or smallest share among the inheritors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator bequeaths 'a share' to Z. The heirs are one son and one daughter (son gets double the daughter). What does Z receive?
A) Half
B) One-third
C) One-sixth
D) One-eighth

Key Takeaway
When a bequest uses the ambiguous term 'share,' Hanafi law interprets it as the smallest heir share, with a minimum of one-sixth.

Bequest of a 'Part' (Juz') of Property
Ruling on bequest of 'a part' of property where heirs choose the amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a bequest of 'juz'' and explain why the heirs determine the amount.

Core Idea
The term 'juz'' in a bequest is so vague that Hanafi law leaves the amount to the heirs' discretion.

A bequest may be expressed with varying degrees of specificity. The word 'juz'' (part) is even more indefinite than 'share'. In this lesson, you will learn how Hanafi fiqh resolves such ambiguity by delegating the decision to the heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ambiguous term
Recognize that the testator used 'juz'' (part) in his bequest. Note that this term is deliberately vague.
Compare with 'sahm' (share) which has a more fixed connotation.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the heirs are told: 'Give him whatever you wish.' There is no fixed amount imposed by law.
This is a default rule due to the total indeterminacy of 'juz''.
3
Understand the rationale
Since 'juz'' has no defined meaning in urf (custom) or shariah, the law cannot fix a quantum. Therefore, it leaves the matter to the heirs' discretion.
The heirs may decide among themselves; if they disagree, the qadi may determine a reasonable amount.
4
Distinguish from related terms
Contrast 'juz'' with 'sahm' (share) where a specific proportion might be inferred from context. 'Juz'' is the most ambiguous level.
Some scholars analogize 'juz'' to 'shay' (thing) in extremity of vagueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أَوْصَى بِجُزْءٍ مِنْ مَالِهِ قِيلَ لِلْوَرَثَةِ أَعْطَوْهُ مَا شِئْتُمْ
And if he bequeaths a part (juz') of his property, it is said to the heirs: give him whatever you wish.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
If a testator uses the term 'part' (juz'), it is even more ambiguous than 'share'. Hanafi law leaves it to the discretion of the heirs to decide what to give, since there is no fixed meaning. This ruling shows the Hanafi approach to vague bequests: if no interpretation is possible, the decision is delegated to the heirs.

Key Terms
جُزْء (juz’): An indeterminate portion, more vague than 'share'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator bequeaths 'a part' of his property. How much does the legatee receive?
A) One-sixth
B) The smallest heir's share
C) Whatever the heirs decide
D) One-third

Key Takeaway
A bequest of an unspecified 'part' (juz') is valid, and the heirs decide how much to give.

Priority of Obligatory Bequests over Voluntary Ones
Order of priority among multiple bequests for religious obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of priority when multiple bequests for rights of Allah are made, distinguishing between obligatory and voluntary bequests.

Core Idea
Obligatory bequests to Allah always come first, regardless of the testator's order.

When a person makes multiple bequests for religious purposes, not all are equal. The law distinguishes between obligatory acts that were due on the deceased and voluntary ones. This lesson explains how the Hanafi school prioritizes them, ensuring that debts to Allah are settled before fulfilling optional wishes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Categorize the bequests as either obligatory (fara'id) like missed Hajj, Zakat, and expiations, or voluntary like supererogatory acts.
2
Understand the default rule
Obligatory bequests are treated as debts owed to Allah; they must be paid first from the estate before any voluntary bequests, irrespective of the testator's order.
3
Apply the testator's order for voluntary bequests
For voluntary bequests only, the order specified by the testator is followed.
4
Test with a scenario
Given a will that lists a voluntary Umrah first and then missed Zakat, determine that Zakat is executed first because it is obligatory.
This demonstrates the hierarchy: obligations override voluntary wishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَوْصَى بِوَصَايَا مِنْ حُقُوقِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى قُدِّمَتِ الْفَرَائِضُ مِنْهَا سَوَاءٌ قَدَّمَهَا الْمُوصِي أَوْ أَخَّرَهَا مِثْلُ الْحَجِّ وَالزَّكَاةِ وَالْكَفَّارَاتِ وَمَا لَيْسَ بِوَاجِبٍ قُدِّمَ مِنْهُ مَا قَدَّمَهُ الْمُوصِي
And whoever bequeaths bequests from the rights of Allah Almighty, the obligatory ones are given precedence among them, whether the testator puts them first or last, such as Hajj, Zakat, and expiations. And what is not obligatory, from it what the testator places first is given precedence.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
When a person makes multiple bequests for religious purposes (rights of Allah), the obligatory ones (faraid) like Hajj, Zakat, and expiations (kaffarat) are paid first, even if the testator mentioned them later. This is because these are debts owed to Allah. For voluntary bequests (like supererogatory Hajj), the testator's own order is followed. This ruling establishes a hierarchy: obligations to Allah override the testator's sequencing.

Key Terms
فَرَائِض (farā’iḍ): Obligatory devotional acts that were due on the deceased, like Hajj, Zakat, Kaffarat.
حُقُوق اللَّهِ (ḥuqūq Allāh): Rights that belong to Allah, including acts of worship and penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat first, then Umrah
B) Umrah first, then Zakat
C) They are equal
D) Only Zakat is done

Key Takeaway
When multiple bequests for rights of Allah are made, obligatory ones (fara'id) take precedence over voluntary ones, regardless of the testator's sequencing.

6 min
Priority of Obligatory Over Voluntary Bequests
Two Urdu usul notes on the priority of obligatory bequests

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi priority order among bequests for rights of Allah and apply it to determine which bequests are paid first from an estate.

Core Idea
Obligatory acts (fard) take precedence over voluntary, then expiations, then other bequests.

When a deceased person leaves unfulfilled religious obligations, the executor must pay them from the estate in a specific order. This lesson explains the Hanafi hierarchy: obligations due to Allah (like missed prayers, Zakat, Hajj) come first, followed by voluntary acts (nawafil), then expiations (kaffarat), and finally any other bequests. Understanding this order ensures the deceased's duties are fulfilled correctly.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the bequests
List all outstanding religious obligations of the deceased. Classify each as fard (obligatory, e.g., missed prayers, Zakat, Hajj), nafl (voluntary, e.g., extra charity), kaffarah (expiation for broken oath, fast, etc.), or other bequest (e.g., a simple charity will).
Only consider debts due to Allah; debts to people take priority before any of these.
2
Rank by religious priority
Apply the rule: fard > nafl > kaffarah > other bequests. This order is absolute; the executor cannot skip a higher category to pay a lower one.
The source specifically says Zakat and Hajj (fard) are more important than expiations.
3
Apply to a concrete estate
Given a list of bequests and a limited estate, determine which bequests must be paid first. Pay all fard bequests before any nafl, then kaffarah, then others. If the estate runs out, the remaining bequests are dropped.
If the estate cannot cover all fard bequests, they are paid proportionally? Not addressed here; assume full payment if possible.
4
Handle insufficiency
If the estate is insufficient, the lower priority bequests are forfeited. For example, if only enough for fard and some nafl, pay fard fully, then nafl from remaining, and nothing for kaffarah or other.
The deceased's liability for unpaid fard remains unless the heirs pay? But the estate's duty ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول: جو فرض ہے وہ اہم ہے، اس کے بعد نوافل، پھر کفارات، اور اس کے بعد باقی وصیتیں ادا کی جائیں گی۔
أصول : زکوۃ اور حج ، کفارات سے اہم ہیں، اسلئے زکوۃ اور حج سے مال بچے تب کفارات ادا کئے جائیں، ورنہ نہیں
Principle: What is obligatory is important, after that supererogatory acts, then expiations, and after that the remaining bequests are executed.
Principle: Zakat and Hajj are more important than expiations, so if money remains after Zakat and Hajj, then expiations are paid, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
These two Urdu notes further explain the order among religious bequests. Obligatory acts (e.g., missed prayers, obligatory Hajj, Zakat) take precedence over voluntary ones (nafl). Expiations (kaffarat) come after voluntary acts? Actually the first note says after obligatory comes voluntary (nawafil), then expiations, then other bequests. The second note specifies that Zakat and Hajj (obligatory) are more important than expiations, so expiations are only paid if money remains after fulfilling those. This hierarchy guides executors.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): Obligatory acts due from the deceased, such as missed prayers, Zakat, and Hajj.
نوافل (nawāfil): Voluntary, supererogatory acts of worship, e.g., extra charity or prayers.
کفارات (kaffārāt): Expiations for offenses like breaking a fast or oath during the deceased's lifetime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A deceased made bequests for: 1) missed Zakat, 2) missed prayers (obligatory), 3) charity (voluntary). Which is paid first?
A) Charity
B) Missed prayers
C) Missed Zakat
D) All equally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school assigns a clear hierarchy to bequests for rights of Allah: obligatory acts first, then voluntary, then expiations, then other bequests.
